Propositions with Personal Subject and Universal Predicate
Author analyzes propositions with personal subject and universal predicate, with and without quantifier on predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a categorical proposition with a personal subject and a universal predicate, with and without quantifier on the predicate.

Core Idea
A universal predicate applied to a personal subject is false when quantified.

The author now examines the third type of proposition from the seven divisions: one where the subject is a specific individual (like Zayd) and the predicate is a universal term. These propositions behave differently depending on whether the predicate carries a quantifier such as 'every' or 'some'. In this lesson, we will follow the author's systematic breakdown to learn how to evaluate such propositions correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The subject is a personal term (e.g., 'Zayd'), and the predicate is a universal term. Note the absence or presence of a quantifier on the predicate.
2
Check if the predicate is stripped of quantifier
If the predicate is used without a quantifier (مجرَّدًا عن السور), the proposition asserts that the individual has the property. It is straightforwardly true or false depending on whether the individual falls under the universal, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer' may be true.
3
Apply universal quantifier to predicate
If the predicate has a universal quantifier (e.g., 'every'), the proposition becomes 'Zayd is every man', which is false in all materials. A single individual cannot be identical to all instances of a universal.
The author states: 'إنه كاذب في المواد بأسرها' – it is false in all materials.
4
Handle negation with 'not one of such'
For 'Zayd is not one of such', the truth depends on the material: false in the material of affirmation (if the individual actually is one of them), true in the material of impossibility, and unknown in the material of possibility.
5
Recognize the pattern for particular quantifier
The passage begins discussing particular affirmation ('Zayd is some…') but the text cuts off. The pattern suggests that truth values also depend on the material, following the author's systematic approach.
The complete analysis would cover all remaining combinations.

Source Text

وأما الثالث من الأقسام السابعة وهو أن يكون الموضوع شخصيا دون المحمول فذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون المحمول فيه كليًا مجرَّدًا عن السور وإنه صادق كما يقال زيد كاتب، أو لا يكون مجرّدًا وإنه كاذب في المواد بأسرها فلا يقال زيد كل إنسان أو كل فرس أو كل كاتب هذا إذا قلنا زيد كل كذا. فأما إذا قلنا زيد لا واحد من كذا فإنه كاذب في مادة الإيجاب، صادق في مادة الامتناع، غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان. وأما في الإيجاب الجزئي فإنه صادق في مادة
As for the third of the seven divisions, which is that the subject is personal and the predicate is not, that is not free from either the predicate therein being universal stripped of the quantifier, and it is true as is said 'Zayd is a writer', or it is not stripped, and it is false in all materials, so one does not say 'Zayd is every man' or 'every horse' or 'every writer' – this if we say 'Zayd is every such'. As for if we say 'Zayd is not one of such', it is false in the material of affirmation, true in the material of impossibility, unknown in the material of possibility. Commentary
This passage begins the analysis of the third main case from the seven divisions mentioned earlier: a proposition where the subject is a personal term (like 'Zayd') and the predicate is a universal term. The author first considers the predicate without a quantifier (مجرَّدًا عن السور), in which case the proposition is straightforwardly true or false based on whether the individual falls under the universal, e.g., 'Zayd is a writer' can be true. If the predicate has a quantifier, then the proposition is false in all materials for universal quantifier, as in 'Zayd is every man' (clearly false). For negation with 'not one of such', the truth depends on the material: false in affirmation, true in impossibility, indeterminate in possibility. The passage then starts to discuss particular affirmation ('Zayd is some...') but is cut off. This shows the structured approach of the author in covering all combinations systematically.

Key Takeaway
Propositions with a personal subject and a quantified universal predicate are false in all materials; with an unquantified predicate, they are true if the individual falls under the universal.

Propositions with Particular Subject and Deviant Forms
Author details truth conditions for propositions with a personal subject and discusses deviant propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the truth value of propositions with a particular subject and quantified predicate across the three logical materials, and identify deviant propositions.

Core Idea
Deviant propositions arise when the predicate includes a quantifier, shifting truth evaluation to the quantified phrase's relation to the subject.

This passage from the logic text examines the third type in the sevenfold division: propositions where the subject is a particular (like 'Zayd') and the predicate is universal. The author systematically unpacks the truth conditions for each possible quantifier combination across the three logical materials (affirmation, impossibility, possibility). This parsing exercise is crucial for understanding how singular terms interact with quantifiers, a subtle area that even Aristotle bypassed. By mastering this, you sharpen your ability to spot non-standard propositional forms and their truth behavior.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the subject type
Identify that the subject in this division is a particular (shakhṣī), i.e., a proper name like 'Zayd'. This is the third of the seven divisions: subject is particular, predicate is universal.
2
Classify the predicate form
Check whether the predicate is a universal term without a quantifier (e.g., 'writer') or with a quantifier (e.g., 'every human', 'some people', 'none of something'). Without a quantifier, it's a standard categorical; with a quantifier, it becomes deviant (munḥarif).
The author says if the predicate is stripped of the quantifier (mujarradan 'an al-sūr), the proposition is truthful in the appropriate matter.
3
Apply truth conditions per quantifier and material
For each quantifier type (universal affirmative/negative, particular affirmative/negative), the passage gives truth values across the three materials: māddat al-ījāb (material of affirmation), māddat al-imtinā' (impossibility), and māddat al-imkān (possibility). Map each combination.
E.g., 'Zayd is every human' is false in all materials; 'Zayd is some people' is true in affirmation, false in impossibility, unknown in possibility.
4
Identify the deviant nature
Note that when the predicate includes a quantifier, the predicate is no longer a simple predicate but part of a complex predicate. The truth condition shifts to the relation of the quantified phrase to the subject. This is the defining feature of deviant propositions.
The author says: 'the consideration of truth transfers to the relation that occurs for that sentence with the subject.'
5
Understand the historical note
Recognize that Aristotle (al-muʿallim al-awwal) did not treat deviant propositions, but later logicians addressed them because of their benefit for implications. This shows the evolution of Islamic logic beyond the Greek tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث من الأقسام السابعة وهو أن يكون الموضوع شخصيا دون المحمول فذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون المحمول فيه كليا مجردا عن السور وإنه صادق كما يقال زيد كاتب، أو لا يكون مجردا وإنه كاذب في المواد بأسرها فلا يقال زيد كل إنسان أو كل فرس أو كل كاتب هذا إذا قلنا زيد كل كذا. فأما إذا قلنا زيد لا واحد من كذا فإنه كاذب في مادة الإيجاب، صادق في مادة الامتناع، غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان. وأما في الإيجاب الجزئي فإنه صادق في مادة الإيجاب فيقال زيد بعض الناس كاذب في مادة الامتناع، غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان. وفي السلب الجزئي إنه كاذب في مادة الإيجاب فلا يقال زيد ليس بعض الناس، صادق في مادة الامتناع غير معلوم الحال في مادة الإمكان. ولنقتصر على هذا القدر من المنحرفات فإن فيه من الكفاية ما فيه. واعلم بأن المحمول إذا كان مقرونا بكلمة السور كانت القضية منحرفة حتى لا يكون المحمول محمولا بل جزءا من المحمول، وحينئذ ينتقل اعتبار الصدق إلى النسبة التي تقع لتلك الجملة مع الموضوع؛ ولهذا يطلق اسم المنحرفات على مثل هذه القضايا وإنها من جملة ما لم يشتغل به المعلم الأول، وفي الحقيقة الاشتغال بمثل هذه القضايا اشتغال بما لا يعنيه، غير أن المتأخرين لما اشتغلوا به لفائدة ما والموافقة من اللوازم فيلزم الاشتغال به إذن وإن كان مستلزما للتكثير.
As for the third of the seven divisions—that the subject is particular without the predicate—it is either that the predicate is universal stripped of the quantifier, and it is truthful, as in 'Zayd is a writer'; or it is not stripped, and it is false in all materials, so one does not say 'Zayd is every human' or 'every horse' or 'every writer'. This when we say 'Zayd is everything'. As for if we say 'Zayd is none of something', it is false in the material of affirmation, truthful in the material of impossibility, and its status is unknown in the material of possibility. As for particular affirmation, it is truthful in the material of affirmation—so one says 'Zayd is some people'—false in the material of impossibility, and its status is unknown in the material of possibility. As for particular negation, it is false in the material of affirmation—so one does not say 'Zayd is not some people'—truthful in the material of impossibility, and its status is unknown in the material of possibility. Let us limit ourselves to this much of deviant propositions, for in it there is sufficiency. Know that when the predicate is conjoined with the word of the quantifier, the proposition becomes deviant, such that the predicate is not a predicate but rather part of the predicate; and then the consideration of truth transfers to the relation that occurs for that sentence with the subject. For this reason the name 'deviant propositions' is applied to such propositions, and they are among what the first teacher did not occupy himself with. In reality, occupying oneself with such propositions is occupying oneself with what does not concern him, except that later scholars occupied themselves with it for some benefit and the correspondence of implications, so occupying oneself with it becomes necessary then, even if it leads to multiplicity.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage examines the third type within the sevenfold division of propositions, specifically when the subject is a particular (like 'Zayd') and the predicate is universal. The author first distinguishes two cases: the predicate is universal but without a quantifier (e.g., 'Zayd is a writer'), which is true in the appropriate matter; or the predicate is universal with a quantifier (e.g., 'Zayd is every human'), which is always false. Then he systematically analyzes truth-values for each combination of quantifier (universal negation, particular affirmation, particular negation) across the three logical 'materials' (māddat al-ījāb, māddat al-imtinā', māddat al-imkān). The conclusion is that such 'deviant propositions' (al-munḥarifāt) arise when the predicate includes a quantifier, making the predicate no longer a simple predicate but part of a complex predicate. Truth then concerns the relation of that whole phrase to the subject. The author notes that Aristotle (al-muʿallim al-awwal) did not treat these, but later logicians addressed them due to practical benefits and the need to account for implications, even at the cost of prolixity.

Key Takeaway
Deviant propositions occur when the predicate includes a quantifier; their truth depends on the logical material and quantifier type, and they were not addressed by Aristotle but are studied in later Islamic logic for their useful implications.

The Categorical Proposition: Components and the Copula
Author defines the categorical proposition and its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of any categorical proposition and distinguish bipartite from tripartite propositions using Islamic logical examples.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has a hidden link (copula) — even when it's not spoken.

In this passage, the author lays the groundwork for all categorical reasoning in Islamic logic. He shows that a proposition is more than just a subject and predicate; it requires a relation (the copula) that connects them. This relation may be explicit or implicit, creating two types of propositions: bipartite and tripartite. Understanding this is essential for analyzing syllogisms and definitions in disciplines like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Find the subject, predicate, and relation in the example 'Man is an animal'. The subject is 'man', the predicate is 'animal', and the relation is 'is'.
The relation may be implicit in a simple juxtaposition.
2
Recognize the necessity of the relation
Why does the author insist the proposition cannot exist without the relation? Because without the copula, the subject and predicate are just two separate concepts, not a complete proposition.
3
Distinguish bipartite vs tripartite
Compare 'Zayd writes' (bipartite, no explicit copula) and 'Zayd is writing' (tripartite, explicit copula 'is'). The bipartite form relies on the mind to supply the copula.
Bipartite can be temporal (writes) or non-temporal (writer).
4
Apply the temporal/non-temporal distinction
Notice that both bipartite and tripartite forms can indicate time (temporal) or timelessness (non-temporal). For example, 'Zayd is a writer' is non-temporal; 'Zayd is writing' is temporal.
Use Islamic examples like 'Salat is obligatory' (non-temporal) vs 'The sun is rising' (temporal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في القضية الحملية ] أما القضية الحملية فإنها مركبة من موضوع و محمول وعلاقة بينهما، كما في قولنا الإنسان حيوان مثلا؛ فإن الإنسان لا يكون موضوعا للحيوان ولا الحيوان محمولا له إلا وأن يكون بينهما نسبة لا وجود للقضية بدون تلك النسبة وهي «العلاقة». ثم تلك النسبة من جملة ما يستحق لفظا يدل عليه لكن قد يقتصر على لفظي الموضوع والمحمول تأويلا على فهم الذهن تلك النسبة اللازمة واللفظة الدالة على تلك النسبة هي «الرابطة»، والرابطة إما أن تكون مذكورة مع الموضوع والمحمول أو لا تكون؛ فإن لم تكن مذكورة كانت القضية ثنائية زمانية كانت أو غير زمانية كما في قولنا زيد يكتب أو كاتب. وإن كانت مذكورة كانت القضية ثلاثية زمانية أو غير زمانية كما في قولنا زيد هو يكتب أو هو كاتب؛ فالثلاثية يكون مصرحا فيها بالرابطة المذكورة.
Chapter: On the Categorical Proposition. As for the categorical proposition, it is composed of a subject, a predicate, and a relation between them, as in our saying 'Man is an animal' for example. For man is not a subject for animal nor is animal a predicate for it unless there is between them a relation—the proposition has no existence without that relation, which is the 'relation'. Then that relation is among what deserves a word indicating it, but one may limit oneself to the two words of subject and predicate, interpreting that the mind understands that necessary relation. The word indicating that relation is the 'copula'. The copula is either mentioned with the subject and predicate or not. If it is not mentioned, the proposition is bipartite, whether temporal or non-temporal, as in our saying 'Zayd writes' or 'is a writer'. If it is mentioned, the proposition is tripartite, temporal or non-temporal, as in our saying 'Zayd is writing' or 'is a writer'. Thus the tripartite has the mentioned copula explicitly stated.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on the categorical proposition (al-qadiyya al-ḥamliyya). The author defines it as having three components: subject (mawḍūʿ), predicate (maḥmūl), and a relation (ʿalāqa) between them. Without this relation, the proposition would not exist. Although the relation is essential, it may not be explicitly stated; the mind grasps it implicitly. The word that indicates this relation is called the 'copula' (rābiṭa). The copula can be present or absent. If absent, the proposition is bipartite (thunāʾiyya), as in 'Zayd writes' (temporal) or 'Zayd is a writer' (non-temporal). If present, it is tripartite (thulāthiyya), as in 'Zayd is writing' or 'Zayd is a writer'. The author makes a further distinction of temporal vs. non-temporal based on whether the copula indicates time, but both forms are valid. This foundational definition sets the stage for analyzing categorical propositions in formal logic.

Key Terms
القضية الحملية
الرابطة

Key Takeaway
Every categorical proposition has a relational component (the copula) that can be explicit or implicit, giving rise to bipartite and tripartite forms.

The Three Ranks of Categorical Propositions
Author introduces three ranks based on copula explicitness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three ranks of categorical propositions based on the explicitness of the copula and distinguish between two-part and three-part propositions.

Core Idea
Propositions are ranked by how explicitly they state the relation between subject and predicate.

In this passage, the author introduces a fundamental classification: propositions can be two-part or three-part, depending on whether the copula is expressed. Understanding these ranks is essential for analyzing the structure of categorical propositions in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three ranks
The author mentions three ranks: (1) relation indicated specifically (explicit copula), (2) relation indicated not specifically, (3) relation not indicated at all (no explicit copula).
The first rank yields three-part propositions; the third rank yields two-part propositions.
2
Locate examples of three-part propositions
The author gives 'Zaid exists just' or 'Zaid is just' as three-part propositions. Note the presence of a copula ('exists' or 'is').
3
Contrast with two-part propositions
The author alludes to 'Zaid is a writer' as a two-part proposition where the copula is implied, not stated explicitly.
In Arabic, two-part propositions lack a separate copula word.
4
Understand the copula's role
The copula indicates that the predicate exists for the subject; it is not part of the predicate itself.
This distinction is crucial for later analysis of propositional structure.

كما أن الأولى من الثلاثيات التامة على خلاف الثانية. ثم الثلاثيات هي التي تصرح فيها بالرابطة كقولنا الإنسان يوجد عدلًا أو هو عدل ، فلفظة «توجد فيها ولفظة «هو» ليست داخلة على أنها بنفسها من المحمول بل ربطها. ولو قرن بها كما في قولنا زيد يوجد لا عادلا كانت الرابطة تدخل على حرف السلب فيكون حرف السلب جزءا من المحمول: فلم يكن العادل بانفراده محمولا بل حمله «اللا عادل» بالإيجاب على خلاف ذلك. فكأنه وصف بأنه غير عادل حتى يصح سلبه بحرف السلب كذلك فيقال زيد ليس يوجد لا عادلًا أو غير عادل فيفرض هنا موجبتان أو سالبتان. فإن قولنا يوجد عادلًا يقابل قولنا زيد ليس يوجد عادلا؛ وهما الموجبة والسالبة البسيطتان. وقولنا زيد يوجد لا عادلًا يقابل قولنا زيد ليس يوجد لا عادلا؛ وهما الموجبة والسالبة المعدولتان. فإن القضية التي محمولها اسم غير محصل أو كلمة غير محصلة تسمى معدولة ومتغيرة؛ فإن أوجبت المحمول كانت موجبة وإن سلبته كانت سالبة. وبالجملة فالبحث عن العدول والتحصيل لا يكون مختصا بالموضوع والمحمول بل هو من الأمور المشتركة بينهما . والاعتبار في كون الحملية موجبة أو سالبة فالإيجاب والسلب لا يكون المحكوم عليه وبه من الأمور الوجودية واللا وجودية. فالقضية الحملية إما أن يكون حرف السلب فيها جزءا من الموضوع لا غير أو من المحمول لا غير أو منهما جميعا، وإما أن لا يكون جزءا لا من الموضوع ولا من المحمول ولا منهما البتة؛ وحينئذ إما أن تكون القضية موجبة أو سالبة كما يقال زيد كاتب، زيد ليس بكاتب» مثلا، والموجبة منهما تسمى محصلة والسالبة بسيطة. فأما إذا كان جزءا منهما، أو من أحدهما؛ فإن كان جزءًا من الموضوع فقط فهي معدولة الموضوع موجبة كانت أو سالبة كما يقال كل ما ليس بحي فهو جماد أو ليس كل ما ليس بحي فهو جماد. وإن كان جزءًا من المحمول فقط فهي معدولة المحمول موجبة كانت أو سالبة كما يقال كل جماد فهو غير عالم أو ليس كل جماد فهو غير عالم. وإن كان جزءًا من الموضوع والمحمول فهي معدولة الموضوع والمحمول موجبة كانت أو سالبة كما يقال كل ما ليس بحي فهو غير عالم أو ليس كل ما ليس بحي فهو غير عالم فتكون الحملية باعتبار العدول والتحصيل على ثمانية أقسام كما مر ، وباعتبار الحصر والإهمال على ثمانية أقسام كذلك، فهي إذن في الموجبات ستة عشر، وفي السوالب ست عشر أيضًا؛ إذ الاجتماع بين البعض من تلك الجملة وبين البعض من هذه الجملة غير ممتنع عقلا لا في الموجبات ولا في السوالب أصلا يمكن أن تكون المخصوصة محصلة أو معدولة الموضوع أو معدولة المحمول أو معدولة
Then, although they do not indicate it specifically insofar as they are themselves, they are not devoid of indicating it specifically in our statement 'Zaid is such-and-such' because the fundamental principle in pursuing a thing is that it relates to another thing. And if it were so, it would not need explicit mention. Thus, propositions with respect to what we mentioned are of three ranks: a rank where the relation is indicated specifically, a rank where the relation is indicated not specifically, and a rank where the relation is not indicated at all. This latter is among the complete two-part propositions, just as the first is among the complete three-part propositions, contrary to the second. Then the three-part propositions are those in which the copula is explicitly stated, like our statement 'The human exists just' or 'He is just.' The word 'exists' and the word 'is' are not included as part of the predicate itself, but to indicate that the predicate exists for the subject. As for the word 'exists,' it indicates the existence of the predicate for the subject in the future; as for the word 'is,' it indicates the existence of the predicate for the subject absolutely. So the copula indicates the relation of the predicate, and the quantifier indicates the quantity of the subject. For this reason, the copula varies with respect to the predicate, and the quantifier varies with respect to the subject. So if the negation particle is attached to the three-part proposition, as in our statement 'Zaid is not just,' that particle enters upon the copula and negates its attachment. And if it is attached as in our statement 'Zaid is not-just,' the copula enters upon the negation particle, so the negation particle becomes part of the predicate. Thus 'just' alone is not the predicate, but rather the predicate is 'not-just' as an affirmative, contrary to that. So it is as if he is described as not-just so that its negation by the negation particle is valid. Similarly, one says 'Zaid is not not-just' or 'non-just,' so here two affirmatives or two negatives are assumed. For our statement 'exists just' corresponds to our statement 'Zaid does not exist just'; they are the simple affirmative and simple negative. And our statement 'Zaid exists not-just' corresponds to our statement 'Zaid does not exist not-just'; they are the modified affirmative and modified negative. For the proposition whose predicate is a non-positive noun or a non-positive word is called modified and variable; if it affirms the predicate, it is affirmative; if it negates it, it is negative. In summary, the investigation of modification and positivity is not restricted to the subject and predicate, but is among matters common to both. And the consideration in whether the categorical is affirmative or negative is that affirmation and negation are not about the subject and predicate in terms of existential and non-existential matters. So the categorical proposition is either such that the negation particle is part of the subject only, or part of the predicate only, or part of both, or not part of either subject or predicate at all. In that case, the proposition is either affirmative or negative, as said 'Zaid is a writer,' 'Zaid is not a writer' for example. The affirmative of these is called positive, and the negative simple. But if it is part of both or one of them, then if it is part of the subject only, it is modified in subject, whether affirmative or negative, as said 'Everything that is not living is inanimate' or 'Not everything that is not living is inanimate.' And if it is part of the predicate only, it is modified in predicate, whether affirmative or negative, as said 'Every inanimate is not-knowing' or 'Not every inanimate is not-knowing.' And if it is part of both subject and predicate, it is modified in subject and predicate, whether affirmative or negative, as said 'Everything that is not living is not-knowing' or 'Not everything that is not living is not-knowing.' Thus the categorical, in terms of modification and positivity, has eight divisions as mentioned, and in terms of quantification and non-quantification likewise eight divisions. So then in affirmatives there are sixteen, and in negatives also sixteen. Since the combination between some of that set and some of this set is not rationally impossible, neither in affirmatives nor in negatives, it is possible for the particular proposition to be positive or modified in subject or modified in predicate or modified
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of categorical propositions (al-qadaya al-hamliyya) based on the presence and placement of the negation particle and the copula. The author begins by asserting that even when propositions do not indicate the relation specifically, they can still indicate it specifically in context. He then establishes three ranks: (1) propositions where the relation is indicated specifically (three-part with explicit copula), (2) propositions where it is indicated not specifically (perhaps elliptical), and (3) propositions where it is not indicated at all (two-part, like 'Zaid is a writer'). The focus then shifts to three-part propositions (ثلاثيات) which contain a copula such as 'exists' (يوجد) or 'is' (هو). The copula's function is to show that the predicate holds for the subject; it is not part of the predicate itself. The quantifier (سور) indicates the quantity of the subject. The author then explains how the negation particle (حرف السلب) interacts with the copula. If it precedes the copula, as in 'Zaid is not existing just,' it negates the attachment (salb al-rabita). If it follows the copula, as in 'Zaid exists not-just,' it becomes part of the predicate, forming a modified (معدولة) predicate. This leads to four basic types: simple affirmative and negative (with no negation particle in the predicate), and modified affirmative and negative (where 'not-just' is the predicate). The author clarifies that a modified proposition has a 'non-positive name' (اسم غير محصل) as predicate and is called 'modified' (معدولة) and 'changing' (متغيرة). He then generalizes: the negation particle can be part of the subject, part of the predicate, part of both, or part of neither. This yields eight types of categorical propositions when combined with positivity/negation (تحصيل/عدول): (1) positive/no modification (simple), (2) modified in subject only, (3) modified in predicate only, (4) modified in both, each with affirmative and negative subcases. Examples given: 'Every non-living is inanimate' (modified subject), 'Every inanimate is non-knowing' (modified predicate), 'Every non-living is non-knowing' (modified both). The author also notes that quantification (الحصر والإهمال) gives another eight divisions, for a total of sixteen affirmatives and sixteen negatives, since the various combinations are logically possible. This meticulous classification is foundational for analyzing categorical syllogisms and understanding how negation and modification affect logical validity.

Simple and Modified Propositions: The Eight Types
Author classifies propositions into eight types based on modification and positivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between simple (basita/muhassala) and modified (ma'dula) categorical propositions, and enumerate the eight types based on the placement of the negation particle.

Core Idea
Modification (عدول) is about including negation within the predicate or subject term, not the overall quality of the proposition.

The author now explains how the negation particle interacts with the copula to produce simple and modified propositions. He then generalizes to show that modification can affect the subject, predicate, both, or neither, yielding eight distinct types. This classification is vital for correctly analyzing the logical form of propositions in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the copula-negation interaction
When negation precedes the copula, as in 'Zaid is not existing just,' it negates the attachment (simple negative). When negation follows the copula, as in 'Zaid exists not-just,' it becomes part of the predicate (modified affirmative).
The copula 'exists' functions as the relation indicator.
2
Distinguish simple from modified
Simple propositions have no negation particle in the subject or predicate terms; modified propositions have negation as part of the term.
For example, 'Zaid is not just' is simple negative; 'Zaid is not-just' is modified affirmative.
3
Identify the four possibilities for negation placement
The negation particle can be part of the subject only, part of the predicate only, part of both, or part of neither.
This gives four structural types.
4
Combine with affirmation and negation
Each of the four possibilities can be affirmed or negated, yielding eight types: e.g., modified subject affirmative, modified subject negative, etc.
The author provides examples: 'Every non-living is inanimate' (modified subject affirmative), 'Every inanimate is non-knowing' (modified predicate affirmative).
5
Apply to the full classification
Remember that the eight types are: simple affirmative, simple negative, modified subject affirmative, modified subject negative, modified predicate affirmative, modified predicate negative, modified both affirmative, modified both negative.
The author also notes quantification divides each into eight, totalling sixteen affirmatives and sixteen negatives.

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of categorical propositions (al-qadaya al-hamliyya) based on the presence and placement of the negation particle and the copula. The author begins by asserting that even when propositions do not indicate the relation specifically, they can still indicate it specifically in context. The copula's function is to show that the predicate holds for the subject; it is not part of the predicate itself. The quantifier (سور) indicates the quantity of the subject. The author then explains how the negation particle (حرف السلب) interacts with the copula. If it precedes the copula, as in 'Zaid is not existing just,' it negates the attachment (salb al-rabita). The 32 Types of Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates 32 types of categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition according to modification (muhassala, basita, ma'dula) and quantification (makhsusa, muhmala, kulliyya, juz'iyya) and determine its place among the 32 types.

Core Idea
Modification and quantification intersect to produce exactly 32 categorical proposition types.

This passage lays out a detailed taxonomy of categorical propositions. The author shows how the presence or absence of a negation particle in the subject or predicate (modification) combines with the four quantification states (specific, unqualified, universal, partial) to yield 16 affirmatives and 16 negatives. Mastering this classification sharpens your ability to parse the logical form of statements in Islamic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish unmodified from modified propositions
If no negation particle is part of subject or predicate, the proposition is 'unmodified' (muhassala if affirmative, basita if negative). If the negation particle is part of subject, predicate, or both, it is 'modified' (ma'dula). Identify which component contains the negation.
The negation particle 'ليس' or 'لا' when attached to a term makes that term modified.
2
Identify the modification type
If only the subject contains the negation, it is ma'dulat al-mawdu; if only the predicate, ma'dulat al-mahmul; if both, ma'dulat al-mawdu' wa al-mahmul. Each can be affirmative or negative, giving 6 modified types plus the 2 unmodified = 8 types by modification.
Affirmative or negative here refers to the quality of the copula, not the presence of negation in terms.
3
Identify the quantification type
Determine if the proposition is particular (makhsusa, e.g. 'Zayd is...'), unqualified (muhmala, e.g. 'Man is...'), universal (kulliyya, e.g. 'Every man...'), or partial (juz'iyya, e.g. 'Some man...'). These four quantification types combine with each of the eight modification types.
The text uses 'hasr' and 'ihmal' to refer to quantification and lack thereof.
4
Combine modification and quantification
Each of the 8 modification types can be combined with each of the 4 quantification types, giving 32 combinations. Since quality (affirmative/negative) is already accounted in the modification classification, the total is 32.
The author explicitly states: 16 affirmatives and 16 negatives, total 32.
5
Practice with examples
Take a proposition like 'كل ما ليس بحي فهو جماد'. Identify: subject contains negation (ma'dulat al-mawdu), affirmative quality, universal quantification. Therefore it is one of the 32 types.
Repeat for other combinations to internalize the grid.

محمولها اسم غير محصل أو كلمة غير محصلة تسمى معدولة ومتغيرة؛ فإن أوجبت المحمول كانت موجبة وإن سلبته كانت سالبة. وبالجملة فالبحث عن العدول والتحصيل لا يكون مختصا بالموضوع والمحمول بل هو من الأمور المشتركة بينهما. والاعتبار في كون الحملية موجبة أو سالبة فالإيجاب والسلب لا يكون المحكوم عليه وبه من الأمور الوجودية واللا وجودية. فالقضية الحملية إما أن يكون حرف السلب فيها جزءا من الموضوع لا غير أو من المحمول لا غير أو منهما جميعا، وإما أن لا يكون جزءا لا من الموضوع ولا من المحمول ولا منهما البتة؛ وحينئذ إما أن تكون القضية موجبة أو سالبة كما يقال زيد كاتب، زيد ليس بكاتب» مثلا، والموجبة منهما تسمى محصلة والسالبة بسيطة. فأما إذا كان جزءا منهما، أو من أحدهما؛ فإن كان جزءًا من الموضوع فقط فهي معدولة الموضوع / موجبة كانت أو سالبة كما يقال كل ما ليس بحي فهو جماد أو ليس كل ما ليس بحي فهو جماد. وإن كان جزءًا من المحمول فقط فهي معدولة المحمول موجبة كانت أو سالبة كما يقال كل جماد فهو غير عالم أو ليس كل جماد فهو غير عالم. وإن كان جزءًا من الموضوع والمحمول فهي معدولة الموضوع والمحمول موجبة كانت أو سالبة كما يقال كل ما ليس بحي فهو غير عالم أو ليس كل ما ليس بحي فهو غير عالم فتكون الحملية باعتبار العدول. والتحصيل على ثمانية أقسام كما مر ، وباعتبار الحصر والإهمال على ثمانية أقسام كذلك، فهي إذن في الموجبات ستة عشر، وفي السوالب ست عشر أيضًا؛ إذ الاجتماع بين البعض من تلك الجملة وبين البعض من هذه الجملة غير ممتنع عقلا لا في الموجبات ولا في السوالب أصلا يمكن أن تكون المخصوصة محصلة أو معدولة الموضوع أو معدولة المحمول أو معدولة الموضوع والمحمول، وكذلك المهملة فإنه يمكن أن يكون من هذه الأربعة، وكذلك المحصورة الكلية والجزئية.
Its predicate is an unqualified noun or an unqualified word called modified and changed; if it affirms the predicate it is affirmative, and if it negates it it is negative. In summary, the investigation of modification and non-modification is not specific to subject and predicate but is among common matters between them. The consideration in a categorical proposition being affirmative or negative is that affirmation and negation are not about the subject and predicate being existential or non-existential. The categorical proposition either has the negation particle as part of the subject only, or part of the predicate only, or part of both, or is not part of any of them at all; then it is either affirmative or negative, as for example 'Zayd is a writer', 'Zayd is not a writer'; the affirmative among them is called unmodified (muhassala) and the negative simple (basita). If it is part of both or one of them: if it is part of the subject only, it is modified in subject, whether affirmative or negative, as 'Everything that is not living is inanimate' or 'Not everything that is not living is inanimate'. If it is part of the predicate only, it is modified in predicate, whether affirmative or negative, as 'Every inanimate is not knowing' or 'Not every inanimate is not knowing'. If it is part of subject and predicate, it is modified in subject and predicate, whether affirmative or negative, as 'Everything that is not living is not knowing' or 'Not everything that is not living is not knowing'. So the categorical proposition according to modification and non-modification has eight divisions, as mentioned, and according to quantification and non-quantification also eight divisions. Thus there are sixteen affirmatives and sixteen negatives; because the combination between some of those classes and some of these classes is not impossible rationally, neither in affirmatives nor in negatives at all. It is possible that the particular (makhsusa) is unmodified, modified in subject, modified in predicate, or modified in subject and predicate; similarly the unqualified (muhmala) can be one of these four, and similarly the universal and partial quantified.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage classifies categorical propositions (al-qadaya al-hamliyya) according to two criteria: whether they are modified (ma'dula) or unmodified (muhassala/basita), and whether they are quantified (mahsuura) or unquantified (muhmala). The author begins by defining terms: a predicate that is an unqualified noun or word is called modified (ma'dula) and changed (mutaghayyira). If the proposition affirms the predicate it is affirmative; if it negates it, negative. Then he explains that the search for modification is not limited to subject or predicate but is common. He then presents the four possibilities for the negation particle (harf al-salb): it can be part of the subject only, predicate only, both, or neither. If it is in neither, the proposition is either affirmative (called muhassala) or negative (called basita). If it is in one or both, we have modified propositions: modified in subject only (ma'dulat al-mawdu'), modified in predicate only (ma'dulat al-mahmul), or modified in both. Each of these can be affirmative or negative, giving 6 types of modified propositions (3 positions × 2 quality) plus the two unmodified (one affirmative, one negative) makes 8 types by modification (عدول). Then he adds the classification by quantification: specific (makhsusa/مخصوصة), unqualified (muhmala/مهملة), universal (kulliyya/كلية), and partial (juz'iyya/جزئية). Actually the text refers to hasr (حصرة) and ihmal (إهمال) implying the four standard quantifications: particular, unqualified, universal, partial. He says each of these can be combined with the four modification types (unmodified, modified subject, modified predicate, modified both), giving 4 × 4 = 16 affirmatives and similarly 16 negatives, total 32. The author emphasizes that such combinations are rationally possible. This is a detailed taxonomy that the student must memorize.

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition is fully defined by its modification (unmodified or modified in subject/predicate/both) and its quantification (particular, unqualified, universal, partial), yielding exactly 32 types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinguishing Modified Affirmative from Simple Negative Propositions
Author explains the difference between modified affirmative and simple negative propositions, focusing on ternary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a modified affirmative and a simple negative proposition in ternary form based on the position of the negation particle relative to the copula.

Core Idea
In ternary propositions, the position of the negation relative to the copula determines modification versus simple negation.

In this passage, the author clarifies a critical distinction in propositional logic: how to tell whether a negation is part of the predicate (modifying it) or negating the copula (making the whole proposition negative). This hinges on the position of the negation particle in relation to the copula, especially in ternary propositions where the copula is explicit. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly analyzing statements in Islamic sciences, where the logical form of a proposition affects inference and definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula
In a ternary proposition, there is an explicit copula (e.g., 'huwa' in Arabic) linking subject and predicate. Locate it first.
2
Locate the negation particle
Find the negation particle (e.g., 'laysa', 'ma'). Determine whether it appears before or after the copula.
3
Determine the type
If negation follows the copula (e.g., 'huwa laysa'), it is a modified affirmative: the negation is part of the predicate. If negation precedes the copula (e.g., 'laysa huwa'), it is a simple negative: the negation negates the copula.
4
Apply to examples
Test with the author's example: 'Zayd huwa laysa bi-basir' (modified) vs 'Zayd laysa huwa bi-basir' (simple). Confirm the pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الفرق بين الموجبة المعدولة والسالبة البسيطة منها بين على الخصوص إذا كانت الموجبة معدولة الموضوع . فأما إذا كانت معدولة المحمول فذلك بتقدم حرف السلب على الرابطة والتأخر عنها كما يقال زيد هو ليس ببصير أو زيد ليس هو ببصير معدولة وبسيطة : هذا إذا كانت القضية ثلاثية. وأما إذا كانت ثنائية فلا يدرى أن حرف السلب فيها لرفع المحمول أو لا للرفع بل موجهة سيما إذا كان المحمول من الكلمات والظاهر أنه يختلف باختلاف اللغات واستعمال حرف السلب فيها كما أن البعض من الحروف الداخلة على الأسماء في لغة العرب أدل على السلب مثل ليس وبعضها على العدول مثل غير. ولو دخلت لفظة «ما» كما في قولك إن «أ» ما ليس بـ«ج» كانت موجبة لما أن معناه هو أن «أ» هو ما ليس بـ«ج» فتقدم الرابطة على السلب في الذهن وإن لم يصرح به. أما إذا لم يكن هناك دليل كان حكم الظاهر أنها ثنائية خالية عن الرابطة فتحتاج إلى ان تقدم ليتحقق ما يجب أن يتحقق في هذا الباب أصولاً؛ وذلك أن الإيجاب في الحقيقة هو الحكم بوجود المحمول للموضوع ووجود الشيء للشيء فرع بالنسبة إلى وجود ذلك الشيء. فإن من المحال أن يحكم بوجود الشيء لما لا وجود له أصلا فإذن كل موضوع في الإيجاب فهو موجود إما في الأعيان وإما في الأذهان. فلو قلتَ مثلا كل مثلث كذا فهو كذا فقد عنّيتَ به أن كل مثلث يوجد كيف كان فهو كذا لا أنه إذا كان معدومًا فهو كذا حال كونه معدومًا . فإن ما يكون معدوما من الأشياء فصفاته [۹۱] معدومة بالضرورة وإلا لما كانت من الصفات بل من الذوات لما أنها / قائمة بنفسها لا بالغير حينئذ.
Then the difference between the modified affirmative and the simple negative is clear especially when the affirmative is modified in subject. As for when it is modified in predicate, that is by the negation particle preceding the copula or following it, as is said 'Zayd is not seeing' or 'Zayd is not seeing'—the first is modified, the second simple: this when the proposition is ternary. As for when it is binary, it is not known whether the negation particle is for negating the predicate or not for negation, but rather it is ambiguous, especially if the predicate is a word. It appears that it differs according to languages and the usage of the negation particle therein, just as some of the particles entering upon nouns in the Arabic language are more indicative of negation like 'laysa' and some are more indicative of modification like 'ghayr'. And if the word 'ma' enters, as in your saying 'A is not B' (inna A ma laysa bi-J), it would be affirmative because its meaning is that 'A is what is not B', so the copula precedes the negation in the mind even if not expressed. As for when there is no evidence, the apparent ruling is that it is binary lacking the copula, so it needs to be preceded to verify what must be verified in this topic as principles; that is because affirmation in reality is the judgment of the existence of the predicate for the subject, and the existence of a thing for a thing is dependent on the existence of that thing. Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between a modified affirmative proposition and a simple negative proposition, particularly when the modification is in the predicate. The author uses the example 'Zayd is not seeing' in two forms: 'Zayd huwa laysa bi-basir' (modified affirmative) and 'Zayd laysa huwa bi-basir' (simple negative). In the ternary proposition (with copula), the position of the negation particle relative to the copula determines the type: if the negation follows the copula, it is a modified affirmative (the negation is part of the predicate term); if the negation precedes the copula, it is a simple negative (negating the copula itself). Then the author discusses binary propositions (without copula), where it is ambiguous whether the negation is modifying the predicate or negating the copula. He notes that in Arabic, certain particles like 'laysa' indicate negation, while 'ghayr' indicates modification. For example, 'ma' in 'innama laysa bi-J' can be interpreted as an affirmative with a modified predicate because the copula is mentally present. If no such indicator exists, the proposition is treated as binary and needs analysis. The author then gives an epistemological principle: in an affirmative proposition, the subject must have existence (in the mind or external world), because one cannot attribute existence to something that does not exist at all. He illustrates with 'every triangle'—the subject 'triangle' must exist in the mind or externally. This ties the logical classification to the ontological foundation of propositions.

Distinguishing Affirmative Privative from Simple Negative
Author explains difference between affirmative privative and simple negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an affirmative privative proposition (موجبة معدولة) and a simple negative proposition (سالبة بسيطة) by analyzing the scope of negation and the existential commitment of the subject.

Core Idea
Affirmative privatives modify the predicate with a negative attribute; simple negatives deny the predicate entirely, and only affirmation requires the subject to exist.

This passage from a classical logic text sharpens a crucial distinction: between a proposition that says 'Zayd is non-seeing' and one that says 'Zayd is not seeing.' At first glance they seem identical, but the logician shows they differ in logical structure, especially regarding whether the subject must exist. You will learn to identify these forms and understand why the first presupposes the subject's existence while the second does not.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the two types in Arabic sentences
Look for the presence of a privative particle like 'ghayr' modifying the predicate (affirmative privative) vs. a negation particle like 'laysa' before the copula (simple negative). For example, 'Zayd is non-seeing' (Zayd ghayr basir) vs. 'Zayd is not seeing' (Zayd laysa basiran).
2
2. Check for the copula (رابطة)
Determine if the proposition is tripartite (has explicit copula) or bipartite (no copula). The difference is clearer in tripartite; in bipartite, ambiguity arises.
In tripartite propositions, the copula helps distinguish the scope of negation.
3
3. Analyze existential commitment
The author states that affirmation requires the subject to exist (either in reality or in mind). Negation can apply to non-existent subjects. Test the proposition: does it assert something about a subject that must exist? If yes, it's affirmative privative; if it merely denies, it's simple negative.
4
4. Apply to Islamic examples
Take a fiqh example: 'Khamr is forbidden' is affirmative. 'Khamr is not permissible' is simple negative. But 'Khamr is non-permissible' (i.e., its attribute is impermissibility) is affirmative privative. Notice the latter implies khamr exists as a substance bearing the attribute.
This distinction matters in legal reasoning about the status of non-existent objects.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموضوع والمحمول، وكذلك المهملة فإنه يمكن أن يكون من هذه الأربعة، وكذلك المحصورة الكلية والجزئية.
ثم الفرق بين الموجبة المعدولة والسالبة البسيطة منها بين على الخصوص إذا كانت الموجبة معدولة الموضوع . فأما إذا كانت معدولة المحمول فذلك بتقدم حرف السلب على الرابطة والتأخر عنها كما يقال زيد هو ليس ببصير أو زيد ليس هو ببصير معدولة وبسيطة : هذا إذا كانت القضية ثلاثية. وأما إذا كانت ثنائية فلا يدرى أن حرف السلب فيها لرفع المحمول أو لا للرفع بل موجهة سيما إذا كان المحمول من الكلمات والظاهر أنه يختلف باختلاف اللغات واستعمال حرف السلب فيها كما أن البعض من الحروف الداخلة على الأسماء في لغة العرب أدل على السلب مثل ليس وبعضها على العدول مثل غير. ولو دخلت لفظة «ما» كما في قولك إن «أ» ما ليس بـ«ج» كانت موجبة لما أن معناه هو أن «أ» هو ما ليس بـ«ج» فتقدم الرابطة على السلب في الذهن وإن لم يصرح به. أما إذا لم يكن هناك دليل كان حكم الظاهر أنها ثنائية خالية عن الرابطة فتحتاج إلى ان تقدم ليتحقق ما يجب أن يتحقق في هذا الباب أصولاً؛ وذلك أن الإيجاب في الحقيقة هو الحكم بوجود المحمول للموضوع ووجود الشيء للشيء فرع بالنسبة إلى وجود ذلك الشيء. فإن من المحال أن يحكم بوجود الشيء لما لا وجود له أصلا فإذن كل موضوع في الإيجاب فهو موجود إما في الأعيان وإما في الأذهان. فلو قلتَ مثلا كل مثلث كذا فهو كذا فقد عنّيتَ به أن كل مثلث يوجد كيف كان فهو كذا لا أنه إذا كان معدومًا فهو كذا حال كونه معدومًا . فإن ما يكون معدوما من الأشياء فصفاته معدومة بالضرورة وإلا لما كانت من الصفات بل من الذوات لما أنها قائمة بنفسها لا بالغير حينئذ.
وأما قول من قال إن للمعدومات صفات حاصلة غير موجودة»، فذلك من جملة ما لا يلتفت إليه، إذ المعنى من الحصول هو الوجود. فلو لم يكن الحاصل عندهم موجودًا فلا مناقشة فيه بل الذهن يحكم على الأشياء بالإيجاب على أنها في أنفسها، ووجودها يوجد لها المحمول أو أنها يعقل في الذهن موجودًا لها المحمول لا من حيث هى في الذهن فقط بل على أنها يوجد لها المحمول عند وجودها في الجملة. ولما كان الإيجاب عبارة عن الحكم بثبوت شيء لشيء كان السلب عبارة عن الحكم بلا ثبوت شيء لشيء وإلا لما كان هذا في مقابلة ذلك فيكون قد يتحقق وهو السلب على الموجود تارة وعلى المعدوم أخرى على خلاف الإيجاب محصلا كان الإيجاب أو معدولًا. وبهذا ظهر الفرق بين الموجبة المعدولة وبين السالبة البسيطة. وأما قول من قال: إن للخلاء أبعاد فذلك من جملة ما لا يلتفت إليه أيضًا؛ إذ الأبعاد أعراض والأعراض لا يمكن وجودها بدون ما هي قائمة به؛ وحينئذ لا يكون الخلاء خلاء بل هو الملاء؛ ولا كلام فيه فلو حكم على الأشياء التي لا وجود لها فذلك على مثال ما هو الموجود منها وكأنه تخيل المعدوم موجودًا وانتهض الحكم عليه وإلا لا يمكن أن يحكم عليه. وكيف يمكن والمعدومات لا يتميز بعضها عن البعض من حيث هي المعدومات ولو تميز البعض منها عن البعض وتعين فلا يكون ذلك منها بل من الموجودات في التخيل وإن كان ذلك المعدومات في الحقيقة.
Subject and predicate, and likewise the indefinite can be among these four, and likewise the universal and particular quantified. Then the difference between the affirmative privative and the simple negative is evident especially when the affirmative is privative in subject. As for when it is privative in predicate, that is by the negation particle preceding the copula or following it, as is said 'Zayd is not seeing' or 'Zayd is not seeing' as privative and simple: this is when the proposition is tripartite. As for when it is bipartite, it is not known whether the negation particle is for removing the predicate or not for removal, but it is ambiguous, especially when the predicate is from words, and it appears that it differs according to languages and the usage of the negation particle in them, just as some of the letters entering nouns in the Arabic language indicate negation like 'laysa' and some indicate privation like 'ghayr'. And if the word 'ma' enters as in your statement 'inn A ma laysa bi J', it is affirmative because its meaning is 'A is what is not J', so the copula precedes negation in the mind even if not expressed. As for when there is no evidence, the apparent ruling is that it is a bipartite proposition devoid of the copula, so it needs to be preceded in order to verify what must be verified in this chapter as principles: and that is because affirmation in reality is the judgment of the existence of the predicate for the subject, and the existence of a thing for a thing is subsidiary to the existence of that thing. For it is impossible to judge the existence of a thing for that which has no existence at all. Therefore every subject in affirmation is existent either in external entities or in minds. So if you say, for example, 'every triangle is such and such', you mean that every triangle exists however it is, it is such and such, not that when it is non-existent it is such and such in the state of its non-existence. For whatever is non-existent among things, its attributes are necessarily non-existent; otherwise they would not be attributes but rather essences because they subsist by themselves not by another then. And as for the statement of whoever said that non-existents have acquired attributes that are non-existent, that is among what is not paid attention to, because the meaning of 'acquired' is existence. So if the acquired according to them is not existent, there is no discussion, but the mind judges things affirmatively as they are in themselves, and their existence has the predicate for them, or that they are conceived in the mind as existent having the predicate, not only insofar as they are in the mind but rather that the predicate exists for them when they exist in some respect. And since affirmation is the judgment of the subsistence of something for something, negation is the judgment of the non-subsistence of something for something; otherwise this would not be in opposition to that, so it may be realized as negation upon the existent sometimes and upon the non-existent another time, contrary to affirmation, whether the affirmation is absolute or privative. And by this the difference between the affirmative privative and the simple negative appears. And as for the statement of whoever said that the void has dimensions, that is also among what is not paid attention to, because dimensions are accidents and accidents cannot exist without what they inhere in; and then the void would not be void but rather plenum; and there is no discussion in it. So if one judges things that have no existence, that is upon the example of what is existent among them, as if he imagines the non-existent as existent and he proceeds with the judgment upon it; otherwise it is not possible to judge upon it. And how is it possible, whereas non-existents are not distinguished from each other insofar as they are non-existents? And if some are distinguished from others and determined, then that is not from them but from existents in imagination, even if those are non-existents in reality.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation distinguishing two types of propositions: the affirmative privative (الموجبة المعدولة) and the simple negative (السالبة البسيطة). The author begins by situating the discussion within categorical propositions, noting that indefinite propositions can be any of the four standard types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). Then he tackles the distinction. For tripartite propositions (those with an explicit copula like 'Zayd is not seeing'), the difference hinges on whether the negation particle modifies the predicate or the copula. When the subject is privative, the distinction is clear; when the predicate is privative, the negation can precede or follow the copula, leading to ambiguous readings. The author gives Arabic examples: 'Zayd is not seeing' can be understood either as an affirmative privative (Zayd is non-seeing) or a simple negative (Zayd is not seeing). In bipartite propositions (no explicit copula), the function of the negation particle is even less clear. The author notes that different languages handle this differently; in Arabic, 'laysa' indicates negation, while 'ghayr' indicates privation. The particle 'ma' followed by 'laysa' creates an affirmative meaning because the copula is implied before negation. When no evidence exists, the default is to treat it as bipartite without copula, requiring further analysis. A key principle is then asserted: affirmation is a judgment that the predicate exists for the subject, and this judgment presupposes the subject's existence in some way—either in external reality (الأعيان) or in the mind (الأذهان). The example 'every triangle' illustrates this: the judgment applies only to existing triangles, not non-existent ones. Non-existent things have non-existent attributes, so they cannot bear a judgment. The author rejects the idea that non-existents have 'acquired attributes', arguing that 'acquired' implies existence. He clarifies that the mind judges things as they are in themselves or as conceived, but always with the condition of existence. From this, the difference between affirmative privative and simple negative becomes clear: affirmation is judgment of subsistence, negation is judgment of non-subsistence. Negation can apply to both existents and non-existents, unlike affirmation which requires existence. The author then refutes a claim that void (الخلاء) has dimensions, arguing that dimensions are accidents needing a substance, so void would not be void. He concludes that judgments on non-existents are only possible by imagining them as existent, but in reality non-existents are indistinguishable.

Key Terms
موجبة معدولة (mūjiba ma‘dūla)
سالبة بسيطة (sāliba basīṭa)
رابطة (rābiṭa)
حرف السلب (ḥarf al-salb)
إيجاب (ījāb)
سلب (salb)

D) non-existent

Key Takeaway
The distinction between affirmative privative and simple negative hinges on scope of negation and existential presupposition: affirmative privative modifies the predicate (still affirmative), while simple negation denies the predicate; only affirmation requires subject existence.

The Condition of Potential Existence in Privative Propositions
Second distinction: privative indicates absence of something that can exist in genus or species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given privative proposition is valid by checking if the negated attribute can exist in the subject's genus or species.

Core Idea
Privative propositions require the negated attribute to be potentially present in the subject's kind.

In distinguishing affirmative privatives from simple negatives, logicians noted that privatives carry a special condition: they indicate the absence of something that could exist in the subject's genus or species. This lesson examines that condition and how it helps us correctly apply privative propositions in Islamic sciences.

Look for terms like 'non-just' (لا عادل) or 'non-speaking' (غير ناطقة) in Arabic propositions. These are privative predicates.

Consider whether the negated attribute can potentially exist in the subject's near genus (الجنس القريب), distant genus (الجنس البعيد), or species (النوع).
For example, for a human, the near genus is animal, distant genus is body; the species is human.
3

If the attribute cannot exist in the subject's nature at all, the proposition is not a valid privative but a simple negative. If it can exist, the privative is valid.

Commentary
This passage presents a second way to distinguish between the affirmative privative (الموجبة المعدولة) and the simple negative (السالبة البسيطة), following an earlier distinction based on negation particles and existential commitment. The author reports that some scholars proposed that the privative proposition indicates the absence of a quality that could potentially exist in the subject, either in its near genus (الجنس القريب), distant genus (الجنس البعيد), or species (النوع). Similarly, calling an animal 'non-speaking' (غير ناطقة) is valid because speech is a potential attribute for animals in their genus, even if not all animals speak. In contrast, calling the rational soul 'non-body' is valid because being a body is not in the nature of the soul; however, the soul is existent. The text cuts off, but the idea is that privative propositions require that the negated attribute be something that could normally exist in the subject's genus or species, whereas simple negatives carry no such condition. This restricts the use of privatives to cases where the absence is of something potentially present.

Distinction between Modified Affirmative and Simple Negative
Author refutes claims about nonexistents and distinguishes two types of negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a modified affirmative (موجبة معدولة) and a simple negative (سالبة بسيطة) and identify the logical implications of each in a proposition.

Core Idea
A modified affirmative is still an affirmation: the negation is inside the predicate, not the copula.

In Arabic logic, not all propositions with negation are negatives. The modified affirmative (موجبة معدولة) contains a negation particle as part of the predicate, making the overall proposition affirmative. In contrast, the simple negative (سالبة بسيطة) negates the entire predication. This distinction is crucial for understanding how syllogisms with negative terms work, as shown in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Read the passage and locate the definitions of الموجبة المعدولة and السالبة البسيطة. Notice that the key difference is whether the negation particle is part of the predicate or negates the copula.
Example: 'Zaid is non-just' (modified) vs 'Zaid is not just' (simple negative).
2
Analyze the refutation of nonexistent attributes
The author argues that nonexistents cannot have attributes because 'acquisition' implies existence. Understand how this leads to the logical point that negation can apply to nonexistents but affirmation cannot.
This refutes the claim that nonexistents have real attributes.
3
Examine the attempted distinctions by others
Some scholars say the modified predicate indicates absence of something that should exist in the genus/species. Understand why the author rejects this in favor of the simple rule: if the negation particle is part of the predicate, it's modified.
Example: 'non-just' implies a nature capable of justice.
4
Apply the distinction to the syllogism example
The author gives a syllogism: 'Every body is non-existing in a subject; everything non-existing in a subject is a substance; therefore every body is a substance.' Check that both premises are modified affirmatives, and the conclusion is a universal affirmative. This shows that modified affirmatives function as affirmatives in inference.
The conclusion is affirmative because both premises are technically affirmative.
5
Test your understanding
Given a proposition like 'The soul is non-body', determine if it is modified affirmative or simple negative, and explain why.
It is modified affirmative because 'non-body' is part of the predicate.

Source Text

وأما قول من قال إن للمعدومات صفات حاصلة غير موجودة، فذلك من جملة ما لا يلتفت إليه، إذ المعنى من الحصول هو الوجود. فلو لم يكن الحاصل عندهم موجودًا فلا مناقشة فيه بل الذهن يحكم على الأشياء بالإيجاب على أنها في أنفسها، ووجودها يوجد لها المحمول أو أنها يعقل في الذهن موجودًا لها المحمول لا من حيث هي في الذهن فقط بل على أنها يوجد لها المحمول عند وجودها في الجملة. ولما كان الإيجاب عبارة عن الحكم بثبوت شيء لشيء كان السلب عبارة عن الحكم بلا ثبوت شيء لشيء وإلا لما كان هذا في مقابلة ذلك فيكون قد يتحقق وهو السلب على الموجود تارة وعلى المعدوم أخرى على خلاف الإيجاب محصلا كان الإيجاب أو معدولًا. وبهذا ظهر والمعدومات لا يتميز بعضها عن البعض من حيث هي المعدومات ولو تميز البعض منها عن البعض وتعين فلا يكون ذلك منها بل من الموجودات في التخيل وإن كان ذلك المعدومات في الحقيقة. ثم إن قوما حاولوا أن يفرقوا بين الموجبة المعدولة وبين السالبة البسيطة بعد الفرق الذي مرَّ ذكره فقالوا المعدولة تدل على عدم أمر من شأنه أن يكون موجودًا في الجنس القريب أو البعيد أو في النوع حتى إن قولنا لا عادل إنما يصح على عادم العدل، وفي طبيعته أن يكون عادلاً أو وفي طبيعة جنسه كقولهم البهيمة إنها غير ناطقة ؛ والنفس الناطقة إنها غير جسم»، وإنهما موجودان في جنسهما. وقوم قالوا أيضًا إن غير البصير هو بإزاء الأعمى حتى لا يصح عندهم الجلد إنه غير بصير غير أن الحق فيه هو الفرق الذي تقدم ذكره: فإن معدولة المحمول عبارة عما يكون حرف السلب فيها جزءًا من المحمول كيف كان. فلو أثبتنا المجموع وهو حرف السلب مع الغير برابطة الإثبات كانت القضية موجبة لا محالة. وأما قولهم إنا إذا أخذنا حرف السلب مع الذي لو انفرد كان محمولاً بعينه؛ ثم أثبتنا المجموع للموضوع، فذلك لا يكون كما ينبغي. فإنا إذا قلنا زيد غير عمرو فهو معدولة المحمول وإن لم يكن الغير وهو العمرو صالحًا لأن الحمل على زيد وإنها موجبة بالضرورة فلا يظن أن حرف السلب في المحمول مما يخرج القضية عن كونها موجبة، فإنا إذا قلنا كل جسم فإنه غير موجود في موضوع ، وكل ما هو غير موجود في موضوع فهو جوهر يُنتج موجبة كلية وهي قولنا وكل جسم جوهر؛ وقد علمنا بأن كل واحدة من المقدمتين، إذا لم تكن موجبة لا يمكن أن تكون النتيجة موجبة بل النتيجة متى وجدت فقد وجدت سالبة على ما عرف.
As for the statement of one who says that nonexistents have acquired attributes that do not exist, that is among the things not paid attention to, because the meaning of 'acquisition' is existence. So if the acquired thing in their view is not existent, then there is no discussion; rather, the mind judges things positively as they are in themselves, and their existence has the predicate for them, or that they are conceived in the mind as having the predicate, not only inasmuch as they are in the mind, but inasmuch as they have the predicate when they exist in general. And since affirmation is the judgment of the establishment of something for something, negation is the judgment of non-establishment of something for something; otherwise this would not be opposite to that. So negation may occur on existents sometimes and on nonexistents other times, contrary to affirmation, whether the affirmation is simple or modified. And by this the difference between the modified affirmative and the simple negative becomes apparent. As for the statement of one who says that void has dimensions, that is also among the things not paid attention to; because dimensions are accidents, and accidents cannot exist without that which they subsist in; and then the void is not void but plenum; and there is no argument in it. For if one judges things that have no existence, that is on the analogy of what exists among them, as if he imagines the nonexistent as existent and then pronounces a judgment upon it; otherwise he cannot judge it. And how can it be possible, while nonexistents are not distinguished from each other inasmuch as they are nonexistents? And if some of them are distinguished from others and become specified, that distinction is not from them but from existents in imagination, even if they are nonexistents in reality. Then a group tried to distinguish between the modified affirmative and the simple negative after the distinction previously mentioned, and they said: The modified indicates the absence of something that is supposed to exist in the near or remote genus or in the species, such that our statement 'not-just' is only valid for one lacking justice, and in his nature is to be just, or in the nature of his genus, like their statement 'the animal is non-speaking' and 'the rational soul is non-body', and both exist in their genus. And a group also said that 'non-seeing' is equivalent to 'blind' so that they do not consider it valid to say 'the skin is non-seeing'. But the truth in it is the distinction previously mentioned: for the modified predicate is that in which the particle of negation is part of the predicate, however it may be. For if we affirm the composite, i.e., the particle of negation along with the other, by a copula of affirmation, the proposition is necessarily affirmative. As for their statement: when we take the particle of negation along with that which, if isolated, would be the very predicate, and then we affirm the composite for the subject, that is not as it should be. For when we say 'Zaid is not-'Amr', it is a modified predicate, even though the other, namely 'Amr, is not suitable for predication on Zaid, and it is necessarily affirmative. So do not think that the particle of negation in the predicate removes the proposition from being affirmative. For when we say 'Every body is non-existing in a subject, and everything non-existing in a subject is a substance', it yields a universal affirmative, namely our statement 'Every body is a substance'. And we know that if either premise is not affirmative, the conclusion cannot be affirmative; rather, whenever the conclusion is found, it is negative, as is known.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative exposition on the distinction between two types of negative propositions: the modified affirmative (موجبة معدولة) and the simple negative (سالبة بسيطة). The author begins by refuting the claim that nonexistents have attributes, emphasizing that 'acquisition' (حصول) implies existence. He then defines affirmation as the judgment of establishing something for something, and negation as the judgment of non-establishment. Negation can apply to both existents and nonexistents, unlike affirmation. This yields the initial distinction: in a modified affirmative, the negation particle is part of the predicate, while in a simple negative it negates the entire copula. The author then dismisses the idea that void has dimensions, using the argument that dimensions are accidents requiring a substrate. He then presents attempts by others to refine the distinction: some say the modified predicate indicates absence of something that should exist in the genus or species (e.g., 'not-just' implies a natural capacity for justice); others equate 'non-seeing' with 'blind'. But the author insists the correct distinction is that in a modified predicate, the negation particle is incorporated into the predicate itself. He illustrates with a categorical syllogism: 'Every body is non-existing in a subject; everything non-existing in a subject is a substance; therefore every body is a substance.' The premises are modified affirmatives, and the conclusion is a universal affirmative, demonstrating that modified affirmatives are indeed affirmative propositions.

Condition for Truth of Modified Affirmative
Author states condition for truth of modified affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a modified affirmative proposition based on the existence of its subject.

Core Idea
Modified affirmatives need real subjects; simple negatives don't.

In this passage, the author clarifies a subtle but crucial condition for the truth of a modified affirmative proposition (like 'Zaid is non-just'). Many beginners mistakenly think that because the predicate is negative in meaning, the proposition can be true even if the subject doesn't exist. But the author explains that it is the affirmative form itself that demands subject existence, regardless of the predicate's modification. This distinction has profound implications for how we reason about non-existent entities in theological and legal discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
Notice the two types: the modified affirmative (موجبة معدولة) and the simple negative (سالبة بسيطة). The modified affirmative has a predicate that is negated in meaning (e.g., 'non-just'), while the simple negative simply denies a predicate (e.g., 'is not just').
2
Understand the existence requirement
The author states that for a modified affirmative to be true, the subject must exist. This is not because of the predicate's meaning, but because affirmation itself requires a real subject to link to.
3
Compare with the simple negative
Contrast with the simple negative: it can be true even if the subject does not exist, because it merely denies something without affirming any positive attribute. Example: 'The phoenix is not seeing' is true, but 'The phoenix is non-seeing' is false because it affirms 'non-seeing' of an existing object.
4
Apply the rule to the phoenix example
The author uses the phoenix (عنقاء) to illustrate. Since the phoenix lacks external existence, any modified affirmative about it is false, while a simple negative about it can be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ فيما يجب أن تعلم في هذا الموضع ] وما يجب أن تعلم في هذا الموضع ، فذلك إن يجب في الموجبة المعدولة كون الموضوع موجودًا لا لأن المحمول فيها، وهو قولنا غير عادل مثلا، يقتضي ذلك بل لأن الإيجاب مما يقتضيه في أن يصدق سواء كان قولنا غير عادل يقع على الموجود والمعدوم أو لا يقع إلا على الموجود. فالسالبة البسيطة أعم من الموجبة المعدولة في أنها يصدق على المعدوم من حيث هو المعدوم بخلاف المعدولة فإنه يصدق أن يقال إن العنقاء ليس هو بصيرا ولا يصدق أن يقال إن العنقاء يوجد لا بصيرا؛ إذ العنقاء لفظ يدل على الشيء الذي لا وجود له في الأعيان بل في الوهم. اللهم إلا أن يحكم عليه باعتبار أنه
Section: [On what must be known in this place]. And what must be known in this place: it is that in the modified affirmative, the subject must exist, not because the predicate in it, e.g., our statement 'non-just', necessitates that, but because affirmation requires it in order to be true, whether our statement 'non-just' applies to the existent and the nonexistent or applies only to the existent. So the simple negative is more general than the modified affirmative in that it is true of the nonexistent inasmuch as it is nonexistent, unlike the modified; so it is true to say 'The phoenix is not seeing', but it is not true to say 'The phoenix exists non-seeing'; for the word 'phoenix' denotes a thing that has no existence in external reality but only in imagination. Unless it is judged by way of
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a definitional statement about a necessary condition for the truth of a modified affirmative proposition. The author begins by stating that the subject in a modified affirmative must exist in reality. This requirement does not come from the predicate itself (e.g., 'non-just' could in principle apply to nonexistents if we consider its meaning), but from the nature of affirmation: to affirm something of something requires the subject to be, because affirmation asserts a positive link between subject and predicate. The author then contrasts with the simple negative, which is more general: a simple negative like 'The phoenix is not seeing' can be true because it merely denies seeing of the phoenix, regardless of the phoenix's existence. But a modified affirmative like 'The phoenix is non-seeing' would imply that the phoenix exists and has the attribute of non-seeing, which is false since the phoenix does not exist. The example of the phoenix (عنقاء) illustrates this: it is a mythical bird with no external existence. Thus, we can truly negate seeing of it, but we cannot truly affirm that it is non-seeing because that would presuppose its existence. The passage ends mid-sentence, possibly continuing the discussion.

Ma‘dulat al-Mahmul: Affirmative Propositions with Negated Predicates
Defines ma‘dulat al-mahmul and illustrates with a categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a ma‘dulat al-mahmul proposition and validate a categorical syllogism composed of such premises.

Core Idea
A proposition with a negated predicate is affirmative because the negation is inside the predicate, not the copula.

This lesson clarifies a common confusion: how a proposition like 'Zayd is not-seeing' can be affirmative. The key is that the negation particle is part of the predicate, not the copula of affirmation. We will then see how such propositions function in a categorical syllogism, demonstrating that two affirmative premises with negated predicates yield an affirmative conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of ma‘dulat al-mahmul
Identify that in ma‘dulat al-mahmul, the negation particle (e.g., 'ghayr', 'la') is part of the predicate. The copula (e.g., 'huwa' or verbal form) remains positive, making the whole proposition affirmative.
Example: 'Zayd is not-seeing' — the predicate is 'not-seeing', and the affirmation is 'Zayd is...'.
2
Distinguish from negative propositions
Contrast with a negative proposition where the negation affects the copula, e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'. In ma‘dulat al-mahmul, the negation is within the predicate term itself.
The author warns against confusing the two.
3
Identify the syllogism premises
Extract the two premises from the passage: 'Every body is not-existing-in-a-subject' and 'Everything not-existing-in-a-subject is a substance'. Note that both are universal affirmative despite the negated predicates.
These are the premises of a first-figure categorical syllogism.
4
Validate the conclusion's quality
Check that both premises are affirmative. According to the author, if either premise were negative, the conclusion would have to be negative. Here both are affirmative, so the conclusion 'Every body is a substance' is correctly affirmative.
This illustrates the rule that an affirmative conclusion requires both premises affirmative.
