Essential vs Accidental Opposition
Author argues that negative propositions are stronger in opposition than affirmative ones, analyzing essential vs accidental opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (dhātī) and accidental (ʻaraḍī) opposition in propositions and evaluate which type yields stronger logical conflict.

Core Idea
Essential opposition is stronger because it pertains to the very nature of the subject, while accidental opposition deals with external relations.

Opposition between propositions can be either essential or accidental. The author illustrates this with the example of good and evil: saying 'good is good' is an essential truth, while 'good is not evil' is accidentally true because evil is not the direct opposite of good's essence but rather a separate quality. This distinction affects how strongly propositions oppose each other.

Guided Path
1
Recall the author's example
The author says 'good is good' is true by essence (dhātī), while 'good is not evil' is true by accident (ʻaraḍī).
2
Understand the basis of truth
The truth of 'good is good' comes from the very nature of good, while 'good is not evil' is true because good and evil are distinct, but this truth is not grounded in the essence of good alone.
3
Apply the distinction to falsehood
Falsehood about essence (e.g., 'good is not good') is stronger than falsehood about accident ('good is evil') because the latter concerns a relation, not the essence.
4
Generalize the principle
In any opposing pair, the one that touches the essence is more fundamental and yields stronger logical conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أشد عنادًا من الموجبة. ولأن قولنا ليس بخير مما يعاده بنفسه على خلاف قولنا هو شر فإنه لا يعاده بنفسه بل بعرضيته أنه يعاند لما يلازمه وهو أنه ليس بشر فتكون معاندته أقدم. ولو كانت معاندته أقدم فعناده أشد فتكون السالبة أشد عنادًا. ثم التضاد في الأمور لا يوجب التضاد في الاعتقاد بل يجب أن تكون متنافية حتى تكون متضادة في الاعتقاد. فلو قلنا للخير إنه خير صدقناه. ولو قلنا إنه ليس بشر فكذلك لكن الصدق في أنه خير بما هو في ذاته؛ إذ الخير خير لذاته على خلاف الصدق في أنه ليس بشر. ألا ترى أن ما يقابله وهو الشر فإنه مباين لذاته فإثبات الخير إذن يتم عليه بذاته وسلب الشر عنه بغيره وبإزائهما كذبان أيضًا مثل قولنا إنه ليس بخير؛ وهو مقابل في ذاته، وإنه شر كذلك؛ وهو مقابل الأمر عارض له والكذب في أمر ذاتي أشد عنادًا بالنسبة إلى ما يكون في أمر عرضي. وأما الظن بأن هذا أشد كذبًا بالنسبة إلى ذلك : فذلك باطل فإنه لا صدق أشد صدقًا من صدق ولا كذب أشد كذبًا من كذب بل بعض الصدق يكون أدوم وبعضه لا يكون أدوم وبعضه في أمر ذاتي، وبعضه ليس في أمر ذاتي وما يكون في أمر ذاتي فإنه أشد عنادًا. ومن جملة ما يدل عليه أيضًا هو أن يقال لا قضية إلا ولها مقابل بل من باب التناقض على خلاف ما يكون مقابلا لها من باب التضاد؛ إذ منها ما لا يقابلها شيء من هذا الباب. فلو قلنا كذا مثلث مثلا وجدنا بإزائه أنه ليس بمثلث ولم نجد كذا الذي هو ضد المثلث. ولو كان كذلك لكان المعاند هو السالبة دون الموجبة. وعناد السالبة للموجبة من حيث هي موجبة كذلك على خلاف عناد الغير. فإن ذلك من حيث هي موجبة بل لأمر عارض. لكن لقائل أن يقول ليس / كلامنا في أن كل موجبة هل يعاندها موجبة أم لا؟ بل يسلم عناد السلب [؟] ثم قال: (وأما المهملات فلا يجري فيها التضاد وقد يصدق معا؛ وكذلك الجزئيات.)
More stubborn in opposition than the affirmative. And because our statement 'it is not good' opposes it by itself, contrary to our statement 'it is evil', for it does not oppose it by itself but by its accident that it opposes what is concomitant with it, namely that it is not evil, so its opposition is more prior. And if its opposition is more prior, then its stubbornness is stronger, so the negative is stronger in opposition. Then opposition in things does not necessitate opposition in belief, rather they must be mutually exclusive in order to be opposite in belief. So if we say of the good that it is good, we affirm it. Why does the author consider 'good is not evil' as accidental opposition?
A) Because evil is not a property of good at all.
B) Because goodness is defined independently of evil.
C) Because 'good is not evil' is true, but its truth is not based on the essence of good.
D) Because evil is the opposite of good by accident.

Key Takeaway
Essential opposition involves the very nature of the subject and is stronger in logical conflict than accidental opposition, which deals with external relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Task 1: For the statement 'Zakat purifies wealth', the opposing statements are 'Zakat does not purify wealth' and 'Zakat is merely a tax'. Which involves essential opposition and which accidental? Explain using the author's criteria.

Contradiction vs Contrariety and the Exception for Indefinite and Particular Propositions
Author argues that negative propositions are stronger in opposition than affirmative ones, analyzing essential vs accidental opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contradiction (tanāquḍ) and contrariety (taḍādd) in propositions and identify which types of propositions (indefinite, particular) lack contrariety.

Core Idea
Every proposition has a contradictory, but not every proposition has a contrary; indefinite and particular propositions can be true together, so contrariety does not apply.

The author distinguishes two types of opposition: contradiction (where one is the direct negation of the other) and contrariety (where two affirmative propositions cannot both be true but can both be false). Using the triangle example, he shows that while every proposition has a contradictory (e.g., 'it is a triangle' vs 'it is not a triangle'), not every proposition has a genuine contrary (e.g., there is no geometrical shape that is the contrary of a triangle). He also notes that indefinite and particular propositions are exempt from contrariety because they can be true together.

Guided Path
1
Define contradiction and contrariety
Contradiction: two propositions that cannot both be true and cannot both be false (e.g., A and not-A). Contrariety: two propositions that cannot both be true but can both be false (e.g., 'all are good' and 'none are good').
2
Analyze the triangle example
The proposition 'This is a triangle' has the contradictory 'This is not a triangle'. The author says there is no contrary for triangle, meaning no shape that is the opposite of triangle in a contrariety sense (like square is not the contrary of triangle; contrariety requires a specific logical relation).
3
Draw the conclusion about negation
Since every proposition has a contradictory (by definition), negative propositions always have an opposing proposition. Contrariety, on the other hand, is not always present; it depends on the content.
4
Note the exception for indefinite and particular propositions
The author states that indefinite (without quantifier) and particular (some) propositions do not have contrariety because they can be true together (e.g., 'Some humans are scholars' and 'Some humans are not scholars' can both be true).
5
Apply the distinction
In practice, when evaluating opposing statements, first check if they are contradictories (negation). If not, see if they are contraries, but remember that indefinite and particular propositions cannot be contraries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أشد عنادًا من الموجبة. ولأن قولنا ليس بخير مما يعاده بنفسه على خلاف قولنا هو شر فإنه لا يعاده بنفسه بل بعرضيته أنه يعاند لما يلازمه وهو أنه ليس بشر فتكون معاندته أقدم. ولو كانت معاندته أقدم فعناده أشد فتكون السالبة أشد عنادًا. ثم التضاد في الأمور لا يوجب التضاد في الاعتقاد بل يجب أن تكون متنافية حتى تكون متضادة في الاعتقاد. فلو قلنا للخير إنه خير صدقناه. ولو قلنا إنه ليس بشر فكذلك لكن الصدق في أنه خير بما هو في ذاته؛ إذ الخير خير لذاته على خلاف الصدق في أنه ليس بشر. ألا ترى أن ما يقابله وهو الشر فإنه مباين لذاته فإثبات الخير إذن يتم عليه بذاته وسلب الشر عنه بغيره وبإزائهما كذبان أيضًا مثل قولنا إنه ليس بخير؛ وهو مقابل في ذاته، وإنه شر كذلك؛ وهو مقابل الأمر عارض له والكذب في أمر ذاتي أشد عنادًا بالنسبة إلى ما يكون في أمر عرضي. وأما الظن بأن هذا أشد كذبًا بالنسبة إلى ذلك : فذلك باطل فإنه لا صدق أشد صدقًا من صدق ولا كذب أشد كذبًا من كذب بل بعض الصدق يكون أدوم وبعضه لا يكون أدوم وبعضه في أمر ذاتي، وبعضه ليس في أمر ذاتي وما يكون في أمر ذاتي فإنه أشد عنادًا. ومن جملة ما يدل عليه أيضًا هو أن يقال لا قضية إلا ولها مقابل بل من باب التناقض على خلاف ما يكون مقابلا لها من باب التضاد؛ إذ منها ما لا يقابلها شيء من هذا الباب. فلو قلنا كذا مثلث مثلا وجدنا بإزائه أنه ليس بمثلث ولم نجد كذا الذي هو ضد المثلث. ولو كان كذلك لكان المعاند هو السالبة دون الموجبة. وعناد السالبة للموجبة من حيث هي موجبة كذلك على خلاف عناد الغير. فإن ذلك من حيث هي موجبة بل لأمر عارض. لكن لقائل أن يقول ليس / كلامنا في أن كل موجبة هل يعاندها موجبة أم لا؟ بل يسلم عناد السلب [؟] ثم قال: (وأما المهملات فلا يجري فيها التضاد وقد يصدق معا؛ وكذلك الجزئيات.)
More stubborn in opposition than the affirmative. And because our statement 'it is not good' opposes it by itself, contrary to our statement 'it is evil', for it does not oppose it by itself but by its accident that it opposes what is concomitant with it, namely that it is not evil, so its opposition is more prior. And if its opposition is more prior, then its stubbornness is stronger, so the negative is stronger in opposition. Then opposition in things does not necessitate opposition in belief, rather they must be mutually exclusive in order to be opposite in belief. So if we say of the good that it is good, we affirm it. And if we say that it is not evil, similarly, but the truth in that it is good is by what is in its essence, for the good is good by its essence, contrary to the truth in that it is not evil. Do you not see that what opposes it, namely evil, is distinct in essence, so the affirmation of good is completed by its essence, and the negation of evil from it is by something else, and corresponding to them are two falsehoods also, such as our statement 'it is not good', which is opposite in essence, and 'it is evil' likewise, which is opposite to an accidental matter. And falsehood in an essential matter is stronger in opposition relative to what is in an accidental matter. As for the opinion that one falsehood is stronger than another, that is false, for there is no truth stronger than truth, nor falsehood stronger than falsehood, but some truth is more enduring and some is not, some is in an essential matter and some is not, and what is in an essential matter is stronger in opposition. And from what also indicates it is that it is said: no proposition but has a counterpart, but from the category of contradiction, contrary to what is its counterpart from the category of contrariety, for among them are those that have no counterpart from this category. So if we say something like 'triangle' for example, we find opposite it that it is not a triangle, and we do not find something that is the contrary of triangle. And if it were so, then the opponent would be the negative, not the affirmative. And the opposition of the negative to the affirmative insofar as it is affirmative is likewise contrary to the opposition of another, for that is insofar as it is affirmative, but due to an accidental matter. But one may say: our discussion is not about whether every affirmative has an affirmative opposite, but rather the opposition of negation is accepted. Then he said: As for indefinite propositions, contrariety does not apply to them, and they may be true together; similarly for particular propositions.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of the strength of opposition between affirmative and negative propositions. The author begins by stating that the negative proposition is stronger in opposition (عناد) than the affirmative. He argues that the statement 'it is not good' opposes its contradictory by itself, whereas 'it is evil' opposes it indirectly. This is because the negation of good is equivalent to the affirmation of not-goodness, which is more directly opposed. He then discusses the distinction between opposition in things (التضاد في الأمور) and opposition in belief (التضاد في الاعتقاد). For example, affirming that good is good is true in itself, while affirming that it is not evil is true but not in itself, since evil is opposite to good by nature. Similarly, falsehood can be essential or accidental; essential falsehood is stronger. He concludes that no truth is stronger than another truth, and no falsehood is stronger than another falsehood, but some truths and falsehoods are more enduring or more essential. He then discusses the relation of contradiction and contrariety, using the example of a triangle: its contradictory is 'not a triangle', while its contrary would be something else, but there is no geometrical contrary to triangle. This shows that the negative proposition (the contradictory) always exists, while the contrary may not. He addresses an objection: the discussion is not about whether every affirmative has a contrary affirmative; it is accepted that the negative opposes. Finally, he notes that indefinite (المهملات) and particular (الجزئيات) propositions do not have contrariety and can be true together.

Defining Modality – Genus and Differentia
Definition of modality (jiha) as a verbal indicator of relational modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of modality (jiha) and explain why modality is accidental to the proposition.

Core Idea
Modality is a verbal accident that indicates how the predicate relates to the subject.

The author opens a new section on modalities by defining the key term 'jiha'. This definition is the foundation for understanding all subsequent distinctions. We will dissect its logical structure to see how it functions as a complete real definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. In the passage, it is 'al-jiha' (الجهة). The author immediately provides its definition.
2
Extract the genus
What broader category does the definition place jiha in? It says 'the word' (اللفظة). So genus = word (a verbal expression).
3
Extract the differentia
What distinguishes jiha from other words? It 'indicates the modality of the relation between subject and predicate' (دالة على كيفية النسبة بين المحكوم به والمحكوم عليه). That is the differentia.
Modality here means how the predicate relates to the subject: e.g., necessarily, possibly, etc.
4
Assess definition type
Does this definition capture the essence? The author provides a real definition (hadd haqiqi) because it gives the essential feature: being a word that indicates relational modality.
5
Note the accidental status
The author later says modality is an accident. Why? Because a proposition considered in itself does not require modality; modality is an added qualification.
This step connects the definition to the broader logical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في بيان الجهات وأقسامها. أما الجهة وهي اللفظة الدالة على كيفية النسبة بين المحكوم به و المحكوم عليه، فإنها وإن كانت منقسمة إلى ثلاثة أقسام كما سبقت قصتها من قبل فقد كانت متعددة حسب تعدد كل قسم من الأقسام الثلاثة. وذلك لأن القضية قد تكون مطلقة؛ وهي المحذوف عنها الجهة قولا وتصورًا كما ذهب إليه البعض، وقد لا تكون بل تكون موجهة بجهة ما. والإطلاق يتقابل التوجيه تقابل العدم والملكة؛ إذ القضية من حيث هي هي مشتملة على ما هو من المقومات لها نحو المحكوم عليه وبه والنسبة على ما هو من العوارض أيضًا نحو الكيفية والكمية والجهة لكن العوارض غير معتبرة في القضية من حيث هي القضية فهي إذن من حيث هي هي شاملة للكل مطلقة كانت أو لا مطلقة. ولا يمتنع أن يكون العارض للشيء ذاتيا له ولغيره أعني المجموع: فالمطلقة كما مر مجردة لا قيد فيها أصلا، والموجهة غير مجردة بل هي مقيدة تقييد
Chapter: On explaining modalities and their divisions. As for modality (al-jiha), it is the word indicating the modality of the relation between the subject and the predicate. Although it is divided into three divisions as mentioned previously, they become many according to the multiplicity of each of the three divisions. That is because a proposition may be absolute (mutlaqa), which is the one from which the modality is removed in wording and conceptualization, as some have held, or it may not be, rather it is modal (muwajjaha) with some modality. Absolute and modal are opposed as privation and possession. For the proposition qua proposition includes what is constitutive of it, like the subject, predicate, and relation, and also includes accidents like quality, quantity, and modality. But accidents are not considered in the proposition qua proposition. So the proposition qua proposition encompasses the whole, whether absolute or not absolute. And it is not impossible that the accident of a thing is essential to it and to others, meaning the composite. So the absolute, as previously, is bare with no restriction at all, while the modal is not bare but rather restricted by a restriction.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on modalities. The author defines modality (الجهة) as the word that indicates the modality (how) of the relation between subject and predicate. He notes that although modality is divided into three main types (as previously discussed), they become many due to the multiplicity of their subdivisions. He then explains that a proposition can be either absolute (مطلقة), i.e., stripped of any modal expression in wording and meaning, or modal (موجهة), i.e., qualified by a modality. The opposition between absolute and modal is like the opposition of privation and possession (عدم وملكة), meaning one is the absence of the other but in a subject that can have it. He clarifies that a proposition considered simply as a proposition includes essential components (subject, predicate, relation) and also accidental features (quality, quantity, modality), but accidents are not essential to the proposition as such. Therefore, the proposition in itself encompasses both absolute and modal types. He then states that it is possible for an accidental feature to be essential for some things and accidental for others, i.e., a composite. Finally, he reiterates that the absolute proposition is bare without any restriction, while the modal is restricted. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing to discuss the types of modalities.

جهة
مطلقة
موجهة
توجيه
عدم وملكة

Key Takeaway
Modality is a verbal accident that qualifies the relation between subject and predicate, and it is not part of the essential structure of a proposition.

Absolute vs Modal – Privation and Possession
Distinction between absolute and modal propositions as privation and possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute (mutlaqa) and modal (muwajjaha) propositions and explain their opposition as privation and possession.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition lacks modality; the modal possesses it – they are opposites like blindness and sight.

After defining modality, the author distinguishes two types of propositions: those stripped of any modal expression (absolute) and those bearing a modal expression (modal). He uses the relation of privation and possession to describe their opposition, showing that modality is an accidental feature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find the terms for absolute (مطلقة) and modal (موجهة) in the Arabic. The author defines absolute as 'the one from which the modality is removed in wording and conceptualization'.
2
Understand the opposition
The author says they are opposed as 'adam wa-malaka (privation and possession)'. This means one is the absence of a feature in a subject that normally can have it, like blindness is absence of sight in a seeing being.
Absolute proposition is the privation of modality; modal is the possession.
3
Apply to propositions
If a proposition is absolute, it lacks any modal word (like 'necessarily', 'possibly'). If it is modal, it includes such a word. The 'proposition qua proposition' does not require modality; it is added.
4
Consider the essence vs accidents
The author explains that a proposition has essential components (subject, predicate, relation) and accidental ones (quality, quantity, modality). Modality is accidental, so a proposition can be absolute or modal without altering its core identity.
This reinforces why absolute and modal are not different types of propositions in essence.

Source Text

Modality and Its Divisions in Propositions
Defining modality and its hierarchical divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify modal propositions by necessity, perpetuity, and possibility, and identify the hierarchy among them.

Core Idea
Necessity is narrower than perpetuity; perpetuity is narrower than general absoluteness.

Modality tells us how firmly the predicate applies to the subject. Is it necessary, always true, or just possible? This lesson unpacks the three modalities and shows that absolute propositions are the default, while modal ones add a qualification. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of modality
Find where the author defines 'jiha' as the expression indicating the manner of the relation between predicate and subject. This sets the stage for all subsequent divisions.
The definition is in the first sentence: 'اللفظة الدالة على كيفية النسبة بين المحكوم به و [المحكوم] عليه'.
2
Distinguish absolute from modal propositions
Note that absolute (mutlaqa) propositions lack any modal expression, while modal (muwajjaha) ones are qualified by necessity, perpetuity, or possibility. The author says the opposition is like that of privation and possession.
Think of absolute as the default state; adding a modality makes it restricted.
3
Identify the three modal divisions
List the three modalities: necessity (darura), perpetuity (dawam), and possibility (imkan). Each has affirmative and negative forms, plus a neutral form.
Necessity and perpetuity share the forms of affirmation and negation, but possibility is opposed to both.
4
Analyze the hierarchy
Recognize that necessity implies perpetuity (but not vice versa), and perpetuity implies general absoluteness (but not vice versa). The author states: 'كل ضروري دائم ولا ينعكس'.
This is a logical subordination: necessary propositions are a subset of perpetual ones, which are a subset of absolute ones (in the broad sense).
5
Differentiate absolute and conditional necessity
Absolute necessity is unconditional and eternal, e.g., 'Allah is living.' Conditional necessity depends on the subject's existence, e.g., 'Man is necessarily an animal' – it only holds as long as man exists.
The condition can be the perpetuity of the essence or something else (subject side, predicate side, or neither).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في بيان الجهات وأقسامها. أما الجهة وهي اللفظة الدالة على كيفية النسبة بين المحكوم به و [ المحكوم ] عليه، فإنها وإن كانت منقسمة إلى ثلاثة أقسام كما سبقت قصتها من قبل فقد كانت متعددة حسب تعدد كل قسم من الأقسام الثلاثة. وذلك لأن القضية قد تكون مطلقة؛ وهي المحذوف عنها الجهة قولا وتصورًا كما ذهب إليه البعض، وقد لا تكون بل تكون موجهة بجهة ما. والإطلاق يتقابل التوجيه تقابل العدم والملكة؛ إذ القضية من حيث هي هي مشتملة على ما هو من المقومات لها نحو المحكوم عليه وبه والنسبة على ما هو من العوارض أيضًا نحو الكيفية والكمية والجهة لكن العوارض غير معتبرة في القضية من حيث هي القضية فهي إذن من حيث هي هي شاملة للكل مطلقة كانت أو لا مطلقة. ولا يمتنع أن يكون العارض للشيء ذاتيا له ولغيره أعني المجموع: فالمطلقة كما مر مجردة لا قيد فيها أصلا، والموجهة غير مجردة بل هي مقيدة تقييد الجهة وهي بيان حال الحكم بالضرورة أو بالدوام مثلا؛ وإنها مقومة لها لا محالة فيكون الإطلاق يقابل التوجيه والإمكان يقابل الضرورة، والإطلاق العام يقابل الدوام فالقسمة باعتبار الضرورة هي ضرورة الإيجاب وضرورة السلب ولا ضرورتهما، وباعتبار الدوام دوام الإيجاب ودوام السلب ولا دوامهما. فالضرورة والدوام يشتركان في الأول والثاني من الأقسام ويفترقان في الإيجاب والسلب، والثالث مقابل لهما ؛ فهذه كلها تحت الإطلاق العام بل ما عدا الإمكان كلا وجملة. وتلك الجملة لا يمكن أن تكون في مرتبة واحدة فيكون بعضها أخص بالنسبة إلى البعض فالضرورة أخص من الدوام لما أن كل ضروري دائم و لا ينعكس؛ يمكن أن يدوم شيء اتفاقا من غير ضرورة [99,] والدوام أخص من الإطلاق العام كذلك. ثم الضرورة [1] قد تكون مطلقة وهي التي يكون الحكم فيها دائم الوجود لم يزل ولا يزال من غير شرط ضرورة دوام الموضوع، وعلى حسب دوامه دوام المحمول كذلك كما في قولنا الله تعالى حي عالم قادر»، فإن الضرورة فيها مطلقة غير معلقة بشرط ما البتة. وما يكون كذلك فذلك دائم الوجود لم يزل ولا يزال وإلا لكان معلقا بشرط. [ب] وقد تكون معلقة بشرط والشرط فيها إما دوام وجود الذات وإما غيره، والغير إما أن يكون في جانب الموضوع أو في جانب المحمول أو لا في جانب الموضوع ولا في جانب المحمول. أما الأول فمثاله قولنا الإنسان بالضرورة حيوان»؛ إذ الضرورة فيه وفيما هو بمثله معلقة بشرط دوام وجود الذات فلا يمكن أن يقال الإنسان حيوان لم يزل أو لا يزال ولا وجود للإنسان أزلا وأبدًا. وكذلك الحال في كل سلب بنسبة هذا الإيجاب كما يقال الإنسان بالضرورة ليس بفرس : فإنه يشبه في الضرورة المشروطة.
Section: On explanation of modalities (jiha) and their divisions. As for modality, it is the expression indicating the modality of the relation between the predicate and the subject. Although it is divided into three divisions as previously mentioned, they are multiplied according to each of the three divisions. That is because a proposition may be absolute (mutlaqa), which is devoid of modality in wording and concept as some hold, or it may not be, but rather be modal (muwajjaha) with some modality. The absolute is opposed to the modal by the opposition of privation and possession. For the proposition in itself includes what is constitutive of it such as subject, predicate, and relation, as well as accidents such as quality, quantity, and modality, but accidents are not considered in the proposition insofar as it is a proposition. So it is in itself inclusive of all, whether absolute or not. It is not impossible that an accident is essential to a thing and to another, i.e., the sum. So the absolute, as mentioned, is bare, without any restriction, while the modal is not bare but rather restricted by a modality, which is an explanation of the state of the judgment by necessity or perpetuity for example; and it is constitutive of it inevitably. Thus the absolute is opposed to the modal, possibility is opposed to necessity, and general absoluteness is opposed to perpetuity. So the division with respect to necessity is: necessity of affirmation, necessity of negation, and neither; with respect to perpetuity: perpetuity of affirmation, perpetuity of negation, and neither. Necessity and perpetuity share the first and second of these divisions and differ in affirmation and negation, and the third is opposed to both. So all these fall under general absoluteness, indeed apart from possibility altogether. And these whole cannot be on one level, so some are more specific than others: necessity is more specific than perpetuity because everything necessary is perpetual but not conversely; something may be perpetual accidentally without necessity. And perpetuity is more specific than general absoluteness similarly. Then necessity may be absolute, which is a judgment whose existence is perpetual, always and forever, without condition of necessity of the subject's perpetuity, and according to its perpetuity is the perpetuity of the predicate likewise, as in our statement: 'Allah is living, knowing, powerful'; for the necessity there is absolute, not conditional upon any condition whatsoever. What is like that is perpetual in existence always and forever; otherwise it would be conditional upon a condition. And it may be conditional, and the condition is either the perpetuity of the existence of the essence or something else. The something else is either on the side of the subject or on the side of the predicate or neither. As for the first, an example is our statement: 'Man is necessarily an animal'; for the necessity therein and in what is like it is conditional upon the perpetuity of the existence of the essence, so it cannot be said that man is an animal always and forever since man does not exist eternally. Similarly, every negation relative to this affirmation, as it is said: 'Man is necessarily not a horse'; for it resembles conditional necessity.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage defines modality (jiha) as the expression indicating the manner (kayfiyya) of the relation between predicate and subject. The author then distinguishes absolute propositions (mutlaqa), which lack modality, from modal propositions (muwajjaha), which are qualified by a modality. He notes that the opposition between absolute and modal is one of privation and possession. Modality includes necessity (darura), perpetuity (dawam), and possibility (imkan). The classification proceeds: necessity has three modes (affirmative necessity, negative necessity, neither), and similarly for perpetuity. The hierarchy is explained: necessity is more specific than perpetuity (all necessary is perpetual but not vice versa), and perpetuity is more specific than general absoluteness. Then two types of necessity are discussed: absolute necessity, exemplified by attributes of Allah (living, knowing, powerful) which are eternal without condition, and conditional necessity, exemplified by 'Man is necessarily an animal' which requires the subject's existence. The condition may be the perpetuity of the essence or something else on either side.

مطلقة

ضرورة

Modality qualifies how the predicate relates to the subject; necessity is the strongest, followed by perpetuity, and both are species of the broader general absoluteness.

Distinguishing Conditional from Unconditional Necessity
Author argues that a certain necessity is conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a necessity is conditional or unconditional and explain why misclassification leads to contradiction.

Core Idea
Misclassifying conditional necessity as unconditional creates a logical contradiction.

In this passage, the author corrects a classification made by Ibn Sina and a later commentator. He argues that a certain type of necessity is not absolute but conditional on the subject's existence or description. Through a clever reductio, he shows that treating it as unconditional would lead to absurd consequences. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating modal propositions in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of necessity
The passage mentions unconditional necessity (ضرورة غير مشروطة) and conditional necessity (ضرورة مشروطة). Unconditional necessity applies regardless of any condition, while conditional necessity depends on a description or time.
2
Find the author's claim about classification
The author states that the necessity in question should be part of the conditional (مشروطة), contrary to Ibn Sina and al-Basa'ir who treated it as unconditional.
The author says 'الحق فيها أن يكون من جملة المشروطة'.
3
Recognize the consequence if unconditional
If it were unconditional, then the judgment about something that is not always existent would be the same as the judgment about something always existent.
This would equate the non-permanent with the permanent.
4
Identify the resulting contradiction
This leads to a contradiction: either the permanent becomes non-permanent or the non-permanent becomes permanent (هذا خلف).
The term 'خلف' indicates an impossible consequence.
5
Understand the reductio argument
The author uses a formal reduction: assuming the necessity is unconditional leads to a contradiction, therefore it must be conditional. This is a valid logical inference.
The reasoning follows modus tollens: if P then Q, but Q is false, so not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الشيخ جعل هذا القسم من جملة الضرورة الغير المشروطة في الشفاء وتابعه صاحب البصائر كذلك لكن الحق فيها أن يكون من جملة المشروطة وإلا لكان الحكم على ما لا يكون دائم الوجود نحو الحكم على ما يكون دائم الوجود، فيلزم أن يكون الدائم لا دائما، أو اللا دائم دائمًا؛ هذا خلف.
Then the Shaykh made this section part of unconditional necessity in al-Shifa, and the author of al-Basa'ir followed him likewise, but the truth is that it should be part of the conditional [necessity]; otherwise there would be a judgment about what is not always existent similar to the judgment about what is always existent, necessitating that the permanent be non-permanent, or the non-permanent be permanent; this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a critical correction of a previous classification. The author states that a certain segment of necessity, which the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) in al-Shifa and the author of al-Basa'ir considered unconditional (غير مشروطة), should actually be conditional (مشروطة). The reasoning is a reductio ad absurdum: if it were unconditional, then we would be making the same judgment about something that is not always existent as about something always existent. This would lead to a contradiction: either the permanent becomes non-permanent or the non-permanent becomes permanent. The phrase 'هذا خلف' indicates that this is an impossible consequence, so the original classification must be revised.

Key Terms

ضرورة مشروطة
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The author of al-Basa'ir disagreed.
D) It is always existent.

Key Takeaway
A necessity that depends on the subject's description must be classified as conditional, lest we fall into contradiction.

Three Types of Predicate-Description Necessity
Author classifies necessity based on the predicate's description into three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of necessity based on the description of the predicate in categorical propositions, and classify given examples accordingly.

Core Idea
Predicate necessity is conditional upon the persistence of a description — absolute, general conditioned, or special conditioned.

In the previous lesson, we saw how necessity can be classified based on the description of the subject. Now the author turns to the predicate side, presenting an analogous threefold division. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting modal propositions in Islamic logic, especially when evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate's description in a proposition
The predicate's description is an attribute that may or may not be permanent. For example, 'animal' is a permanent description of 'human', while 'moving' is a transient description of 'animal'.
2
Determine if the description endures with the subject's essence
If the description is inseparable (like 'animal' for 'human'), the proposition is absolute necessary — the subject can never be devoid of the predicate.
3
If the description does not endure, check the condition of the necessity
If the necessity lasts only as long as the description (e.g., 'as long as it is moving'), it is general conditioned. If it is explicitly not permanent (e.g., 'not always but as long as it is moving'), it is special conditioned.
4
Recognize the inclusive hierarchy
The general conditioned encompasses both the special conditioned and the absolute necessary. This mirrors the previous classification for subject-side necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث وهو الذي تكون الضرورة فيه بحسب وصف المحمول فذلك الوصف إما أن يدوم بدوام ذات الموضوع؛ وحينئذ يدوم المحمول بدوام ذات الموضوع إذا كان دائمًا بدوام ذلك الوصف كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان حيوان ما دام حيوانا»، وهذه القضية من قبيل الضرورية المطلقة أيضًا لامتناع خلو الموضوع عن المحمول. وإما أن لا يدوم؛ وحينئذ إما أن يكون مشروطًا بشرط الدوام لا غير كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل حيوان متحرك ما دام متحركا»، وهذه هي المشروطة العامة من جانب المحمول، وإما أن لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا، كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل حيوان متحرك لا دائما بل ما دام متحركا»، وهذه هي المشروطة الخاصة من جانب المحمول. فالمشروط العامة تعم المشروط الخاصة والضرورية التي تقدم ذكرها كذلك كما مرَّ من قبل.
As for the third [type], which is where necessity is according to the description of the predicate: that description either endures with the endurance of the subject's essence; then the predicate endures with the endurance of the subject's essence if it is permanent with the endurance of that description, as in our saying 'Necessarily every human is an animal as long as he is an animal' — this proposition is also of the absolute necessary type because of the impossibility of the subject being devoid of the predicate. [The description] may also not endure; then it is either conditioned by the condition of duration only, as in our saying 'Necessarily every animal is moving as long as it is moving' — this is the general conditioned from the side of the predicate; or it may not be, but rather conditioned by the condition of non-duration as well, as in our saying 'Necessarily every animal is moving not always but as long as it is moving' — this is the special conditioned from the side of the predicate. So the general conditioned encompasses the special conditioned and the necessary proposition mentioned before as well, as has passed.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage mirrors the previous classification but now for necessity based on the description of the predicate. The same three sub-types appear: First, if the predicate's description endures with the subject's essence (e.g., 'animal' as a description of 'human' is permanent), then the proposition is absolute necessary. Second, if the description does not endure (e.g., 'moving' is not a permanent description for 'animal' because animals may be at rest), then we have the 'general conditioned' where the necessity holds only for the duration of the description. Third, the 'special conditioned' where the necessity is explicitly not permanent. The author notes that the general conditioned includes the special conditioned and the absolute necessary proposition, just as in the previous division. The phrase 'كما مرَّ من قبل' refers back to the similar classification of the subject-side necessity.

Key Terms
وصف المحمول
المشروطة العامة من جانب المحمول
المشروطة الخاصة من جانب المحمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Necessarily every human is an animal as long as he is an animal.
B) Necessarily every animal is moving as long as it is moving.
C) Necessarily every animal is moving not always but as long as it is moving.
D) Necessarily every human is an animal.

Key Takeaway
The general conditioned proposition from the predicate side includes both the absolute necessary and the special conditioned, mirroring the hierarchy of subject-side necessity.

Temporal Necessity: Time-Specific and Indefinite
Classifies temporal necessity into four sub-types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify temporal modal propositions into their four sub-types based on time specificity and duration condition.

Core Idea
Temporal necessity splits into four types depending on whether the time is specific or indefinite and whether permanence is negated.

In this passage, the author completes his classification of modal propositions by examining those where necessity is tied to time. You have already learned about propositions with necessity based on subject or predicate description. Now we explore four distinct temporal modalities that are crucial for understanding how necessity can be qualified by time in Islamic logic. This classification has direct applications in theological and legal reasoning where time-bound necessity matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
The passage splits temporal necessity into two broad branches: specific time (وقت معين) and indefinite time (وقت غير معين). Locate these phrases in the Arabic to see the primary split.
2
Distinguish general vs. special within each branch
For each branch, the author adds a further condition: whether the proposition includes a negation of permanence ('لا دائما'). The general type does not negate permanence; the special type does.
The 'general' types allow the possibility of always being true, while 'special' explicitly denies it.
3
Apply the pattern to the examples
Match each Arabic example to its type: the moon (القمر) examples are time-specific; the human (الإنسان) examples are indefinite. The addition of 'لا دائما' changes general to special.
For instance, 'كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض' is general temporal, while adding 'لا دائما' makes it special temporal.
4
Recognize the non-temporal case
The passage ends with a contrasting example 'كل مستيقظ نائم' which is not temporal necessity at all. Understand why this is included: to show the limit of the classification.
This example is false and illustrates that not all temporal statements are necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع وهو الذي لا يكون بحسب وصف الموضوع ولا يكون بحسب وصف المحمول كذلك. فالضرورة فيه إما أن تكون بحسب وقت معين؛ وإنه قد يكون مشروطًا بشرط ذلك الوقت لا غير كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، وهذه هي الوقتية العامة، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف لا دائما بل وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس»، وهذه هي الوقتية الخاصة، وإما أن تكون بحسب وقت غير معين كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت من الأوقات»، وهذه هي المنتشرة العامة. وإن كان مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا فهي المنتشرة الخاصة كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس لا دائما بل في بعض من الأوقات»، وهذا إذا كانت الضرورة فيه بحسب الوقت، فأما إذا لم يكن فذلك مثل قولنا «كل مستيقظ نائم».
As for the fourth [type], which is neither according to the description of the subject nor according to the description of the predicate. The necessity in it is either according to a specific time; and it may be conditioned by the condition of that time only, as in our saying 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of the Earth's interposition between it and the sun' — this is the general temporal; or it may not be, but rather conditioned by the condition of non-duration as well, as in our saying 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed not always but at the time of the Earth's interposition between it and the sun' — this is the special temporal. Or [the necessity] may be according to an indefinite time, as in our saying 'Necessarily every human breathes at some time' — this is the general extended. And if it is conditioned by the condition of non-duration as well, then it is the special extended, as in our saying 'Necessarily every human breathes not always but at some of the times' — and this is if the necessity in it is according to time. As for when it is not, that is like our saying 'Every awake person is asleep'.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth division of modal propositions: those where necessity is based on time, not on a description of subject or predicate. Two main categories: (1) Specific time, e.g., a lunar eclipse occurring only when the Earth is between the sun and moon. The 'general temporal' states the necessity at that time without negating permanence, while the 'special temporal' explicitly says 'not always'. (2) Indefinite time, e.g., every human breathes at some unspecified time. The 'general extended' simply asserts necessity at some time, while the 'special extended' adds that it is not always but only at some times. The author also mentions a case where the necessity is not based on time at all, giving an example: 'Every awake person is asleep' — this is clearly false and not a necessary proposition; it illustrates that not all temporal statements are necessary. This classification highlights the various ways temporal conditions can affect modal propositions.

Key Terms
الوقتية العامة (al-waqtiyya al-'amma): A proposition where necessity holds at a specific time, without negating permanence.
الوقتية الخاصة (al-waqtiyya al-khassa): A proposition where necessity holds at a specific time, explicitly not always.
المنتشرة العامة (al-muntashira al-'amma): A proposition where necessity holds at some indefinite time, without negating permanence.
المنتشرة الخاصة (al-muntashira al-khassa): A proposition where necessity holds at some indefinite time, explicitly not always.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Temporal modal propositions are classified by time specificity (specific/indefinite) and the presence of a non-duration condition, yielding four distinct types.

Prior vs Posterior Necessity and Non-Necessity
Author classifies necessity as prior or posterior, and treats non-necessity analogously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prior necessity (darura sabiqa) and posterior necessity (darura lahiqa) and identify the corresponding types of non-necessity.

Core Idea
Necessity can be prior or posterior; non-necessity mirrors its divisions.

In modal logic, necessity is not a single concept. The author explains that in all forms of necessity discussed earlier, necessity is prior—meaning the necessity condition is attached to the subject before the proposition is considered true. However, one type—necessity conditioned by the predicate's description—is posterior, arising from the predicate. Similarly, non-necessity (la darura) is not one but multiple, corresponding to each type of necessity. Understanding this distinction helps in analyzing the modality of propositions in Islamic sciences such as kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of necessity
The text states that 'necessity in all these forms is prior necessity except necessity with condition from the side of the predicate, for it is posterior necessity.' So there are two categories: prior and posterior.
Prior necessity is attached to the subject; posterior necessity arises from the predicate.
2
Understand prior necessity
Prior necessity means the necessity is asserted before the proposition's truth, typically related to the subject's essence or description. For example, 'Every human is rational by necessity' would have prior necessity.
3
Understand posterior necessity
Posterior necessity depends on the predicate's description. For example, 'Every writer is necessarily moving his hand while writing'—the necessity follows from the predicate activity.
The text says it is 'later than it' (the predicate).
4
Apply the same classification to non-necessity
Non-necessity (la darura) is the counterpart of necessity and is multiple according to the multiplicity in necessity. So there are prior and posterior non-necessities as well.
Example: 'Every human is asleep, not necessarily' is absolute non-necessity (existential, non-necessary).
5
Recognize absolute non-necessity
Absolute non-necessity is like 'Every human is asleep, not necessarily'—it may be true accidentally, without necessity. This shows that non-necessity can be existential and not required to be always true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الضرورة في جميع هذه الصور ضرورة سابقة إلا الضرورة بالشرط من جانب المحمول؛ إذ هي ضرورة لاحقة ومتأخرة عنه. وأما اللا ضرورة فإنها من حيث إنها في مقابلة الضرورة متعددة حسب التعدد في الضرورة، وإنها إذا كانت مطلقة كما في قولنا كل إنسان نائم لا بالضرورة، وهي الوجودية اللا ضرورية فقد يكون، وقد لا يكون فإن من الممكن أن يدوم الشيء اتفاقا من غير ضرورة كما مر . ثم المشاركات والمتابينات من اللوازم فيها بل فيها وفي
Then necessity in all these forms is prior necessity except the necessity with condition from the side of the predicate; for it is posterior necessity and later than it. As for non-necessity, it, inasmuch as it is the counterpart of necessity, is multiple according to the multiplicity in necessity. And if it is absolute, as in our saying 'Every human is asleep, not necessarily' — and it is the existential non-necessary — it may be and may not be, for it is possible for a thing to endure accidentally without necessity, as has passed. Then the commonalities and differences in the concomitants are in them, rather in them and in...

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of modal propositions by noting the priority of necessity. In all the types discussed (subject-side, time-based, etc.), necessity is prior (i.e., the necessity is asserted as a condition for the truth of the proposition). However, in the case of necessity conditioned by the predicate's description, the necessity is posterior (i.e., it follows from the predicate's description). The author then turns to non-necessity (لا ضرورة), which is the negation of necessity. It too has multiple forms corresponding to the types of necessity. For example, absolute non-necessity is 'not necessary' simpliciter, like 'Every human is asleep' (which is not necessarily true; it may happen accidentally). The statement can be true or false depending on circumstances. The passage ends with a reference to 'commonalities and differences in the concomitants' (المشاركات والمتابينات من اللوازم), but the text is cut off; the analysis presumably continues.

Types of Necessary Propositions: Mashruta, Waqtiyya, Munatsira
Classifies necessary propositions into temporal and extended subcategories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four subcategories of necessary propositions (general/specific temporal and extended) and explain the basis of their necessity.

Core Idea
Necessity in propositions is classified by its basis: subject, predicate, or time.

In Islamic logic, necessity is not monolithic. This passage from the Mantiq Kabir breaks down necessary propositions based on whether the necessity stems from the subject's description, the predicate's description, or a temporal condition. You'll learn to parse these modal nuances, which are essential for evaluating syllogisms in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three original types
The passage begins by affirming that the general conditional (mashruta 'amma) includes the specific conditional and the absolute necessary (darura mutlaqa). Understand that these three types (general conditional, specific conditional, absolute necessary) are distinguished by whether necessity is tied to the subject description.
The 'absolute necessary' here refers to propositions like 'Every human is necessarily an animal'—necessity based on essence.
2
Understand the fourth type: temporal/extended
The fourth type is when necessity is not based on subject or predicate description but on time. This splits into temporal (specific time) and extended (indefinite time). Note the two subcategories: general (conditioned only by time) and specific (conditioned also by non-permanence).
Example: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of earth's interposition' is general temporal; adding 'not always' makes it specific temporal.
3
Analyze the extended examples
The extended (munatsira) uses indefinite time: 'Every human breathes at some time' (general extended). The specific extended adds 'not always'. Recognize that these propositions are still necessary, but the necessity is over an indefinite period.
Contrast with the non-necessary (la darura) which is merely possible.
4
Distinguish prior vs. posterior necessity
The passage notes that in all these forms, necessity is 'prior' (sabiqa) except when necessity is conditioned by the predicate, which is 'posterior' (lahiqa). Understand the difference: prior necessity attaches to the subject's essence; posterior necessity depends on the predicate.
For example, 'Every writer necessarily moves his hand'—the necessity stems from the predicate (writing) not the subject (writer).
5
Parse the non-necessity discussion
Finally, non-necessity (la darura) is contrasted. It is the opposite of necessity but can also be classified. The passage gives an absolute example: 'Every human sleeps not necessarily' meaning it is possible (may or may not happen). Understand that non-necessity is not simply denial but a modal category.
This connects to the modal square of opposition in later discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمشروط العامة تعم المشروط الخاصة والضرورية التي تقدم ذكرها كذلك كما مرَّ من قبل واعلم أن هذه الثلاثة إنما تعتبر بحسب الضرورة لا بحسب نفس الحكم.
وأما الرابع وهو الذي لا يكون بحسب وصف الموضوع ولا يكون بحسب وصف المحمول كذلك. فالضرورة فيه إما أن تكون بحسب وقت معين؛ وإنه قد يكون مشروطًا بشرط ذلك الوقت لا غير كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، وهذه هي الوقتية العامة، وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل قمر منخسف لا دائما بل وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس، وهذه هي الوقتية الخاصة، وإما أن تكون بحسب وقت غير معين كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت من الأوقات، وهذه هي المنتشرة العامة. وإن كان مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا فهي المنتشرة الخاصة كما في قولنا بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس لا دائما بل في بعض من الأوقات، وهذا إذا كانت الضرورة فيه بحسب الوقت، فأما إذا لم يكن فذلك مثل قولنا «كل مستيقظ نائم».
ثم الضرورة في جميع هذه الصور ضرورة سابقة إلا الضرورة بالشرط من جانب المحمول؛ إذ هي ضرورة لاحقة ومتأخرة عنه. وأما اللا ضرورة فإنها من حيث إنها في مقابلة الضرورة متعددة حسب التعدد في الضرورة، وإنها إذا كانت مطلقة كما في قولنا كل إنسان نائم لا بالضرورة، وهي الوجودية اللا ضرورية فقد يكون، وقد لا يكون فإن من الممكن أن يدوم الشيء اتفاقا من غير ضرورة كما مر.
Thus the general conditional (mashruta 'amma) includes the specific conditional and the necessary that was mentioned earlier, as previously. Know that these three are considered according to necessity, not according to the judgment itself. As for the fourth—which is not according to the description of the subject nor according to the description of the predicate—necessity in it is either according to a specific time: it may be conditioned only by that time, as in our statement 'necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun'—this is the general temporal (waqtiyya 'amma); and it may not be, but be conditioned by non-permanence as well, as in 'necessarily every moon is eclipsed not always but at the time of the earth's interposition'—this is the specific temporal. Or it may be according to a non-specific time, as in 'necessarily every human breathes at some time'—this is the general extended (munatsira 'amma). If it is also conditioned by non-permanence, it is the specific extended, as in 'necessarily every human breathes not always but at some time'—this if necessity is according to time. But if not, then it is like our statement 'every awake person is asleep'. Then necessity in all these forms is prior necessity except for necessity conditioned on the side of the predicate, for it is posterior and subsequent. As for non-necessity (la darura), it is multiple according to the multiplicity of necessity, since it is opposite to necessity. If it is absolute, as in 'every human sleeps not necessarily'—that is the existential non-necessary—it may be or may not be, for it is possible that something persists accidentally without necessity, as mentioned.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage defines four types of necessary propositions based on whether the necessity is tied to the description of the subject, the predicate, or neither (i.e., time). The author first mentions that the general conditional includes the specific conditional and the previously mentioned necessary ones. Then introduces the fourth type: propositions where necessity is not due to subject or predicate descriptions but due to a time condition. This fourth type splits into temporal (waqtiyya) with definite time (general vs. specific) and extended (munatsira) with indefinite time (general vs. specific). Examples: 'Every moon is eclipsed when earth interposes' (general temporal) and 'Every human breathes at some time' (general extended). Specific variants add 'not always'. Then the author notes that necessity in all these is 'prior necessity' except for predicate-conditioned necessity, which is 'posterior'. Finally, non-necessity (la darura) is discussed: it is the opposite of necessity and can be absolute (i.e., possible) like 'every human sleeps not necessarily' meaning it may or may not happen. The classification is important for modal logic in Islamic thought, used to analyze propositions for syllogistic validity.

Necessary propositions are classified by the basis of necessity: subject-description (conditional), predicate-description, or time (temporal/extended), with further subdivisions for generality and permanence.

Classification of Perpetual Propositions: Absolute and Conditioned
Analyzes perpetual propositions into absolute, condition-by-subject, and condition-by-predicate with subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify perpetual propositions into absolute, general 'urfiya, and specific 'urfiya, and identify the condition type for each.

Core Idea
Perpetual propositions are not always eternal; they can be conditioned by subject or predicate descriptions.

The concept of perpetuity (dawam) in Islamic logic is more nuanced than simple 'always'. This passage from the logic text breaks down perpetual propositions into categories based on whether the perpetuity is tied to the essence of the subject, a description of the subject, or a description of the predicate. Understanding this classification is essential for correctly evaluating modal arguments and conversions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
The passage first splits perpetuity into absolute (unconditioned) and conditioned. Note the three possible sources for the condition: subject side, predicate side, or neither.
The classification is systematic: the condition's source determines the type.
2
Analyze the subject-side condition
When the condition is from the subject's description, check if that description persists with the subject's essence. If yes, it reduces to absolute perpetual. If not, it becomes 'urfiya (general or specific).
This step requires distinguishing between essence and accidental description.
3
Distinguish general and specific 'urfiya
General 'urfiya: predicate holds as long as the description exists, e.g., 'wine is intoxicating as long as it is wine'. Specific 'urfiya adds that it does not hold always, e.g., 'not always but as long as it is wine'.
The general 'urfiya includes the specific 'urfiya and the absolute perpetual.
4
Apply to examples
Given a proposition, determine which type it is by checking what exactly the perpetuity depends on. Practice with the examples in the passage.
This skill is crucial for later lessons on modal conversion and syllogistic validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدوام فإنه قد يكون مطلقا وقد لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط؛ وذلك الشرط إما أن يكون من جانب الموضوع، أو من جانب المحمول، أو لا من جانب الموضوع ولا من جانب المحمول. [أ] أما الأول فهو الذي يكون الدوام فيه بحسب دوام ذات الموضوع كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان دائمًا، وهذه القضية هي الدائمة المطلقة فيكون المحمول موجودًا ما دام الموضوع موجودًا. [ب] وأما الثاني وهو الذي يكون الدوام فيه بحسب وصف الموضوع، فذلك الوصف إما أن يدوم بدوام ذات الموضوع؛ وحينئذ يدوم المحمول بدوام ذات الموضوع إذا كان دائمًا بدوام ذلك الوصف كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان ما دام إنسانًا وهذه القضية من قبيل الدائمة المطلقة لما أن المحمول موجود ما دام الموضوع موجودًا. وإما أن لا يدوم وحينئذ إما أن يكون مشروطًا بشرط الدوام لا غير كما في قولنا كل خمر مسكر ما دام خمرا وهذه هي العرفية العامة؛ وقد تسمى مطلقة منعكسة أيضًا لما أن السالبة منها تنعكس دون السالبة من المطلقة العامة. وإما أن لا يكون بل يكون مشروطًا بشرط اللا دوام أيضًا كما في قولنا كل خمر مسكر لا دائما بل ما دام خمرًا، وهذه هي العرفية الخاصة، فالعرفية العامة تعم العرفية الخاصة والمطلقة التي تقدم ذكرها. [ت] وأما الثالث وهو الذي يكون الدوام فيه بحسب وصف المحمول، فذلك الوصف إما أن يدوم بدوام ذات الموضوع؛ وحينئذ يدوم المحمول بدوام ذات الموضوع كما في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان ما دام حيوانا، وهذه القضية من قبيل الدائمة المطلقة أيضًا. وإما لا يدوم وحينئذ إما أن يكون
As for perpetuity (dawam), it may be absolute or it may not be, but be conditioned by a condition. That condition is either from the side of the subject, or from the side of the predicate, or from neither side. [A] The first: perpetuity is according to the permanence of the subject's essence, as in our saying 'every human is an animal perpetually'—this proposition is the absolute perpetual (da'ima mutlaqa); thus the predicate exists as long as the subject exists. [B] The second: perpetuity is according to the description of the subject. That description either persists with the persistence of the subject's essence; then the predicate persists with the persistence of the subject's essence if it is perpetual by the persistence of that description, as in 'every human is an animal as long as he is a human'—this proposition is of the kind of absolute perpetual because the predicate exists as long as the subject exists. Or it does not persist; then either it is conditioned only by the condition of perpetuity, as in 'every wine is intoxicating as long as it is wine'—this is the general 'urfiya; it is also called absolute converted because its negative converts while the negative of the general absolute does not. Or it is conditioned by non-perpetuity as well, as in 'every wine is intoxicating not always but as long as it is wine'—this is the specific 'urfiya. Thus the general 'urfiya includes the specific 'urfiya and the absolute that was mentioned earlier. [C] The third: perpetuity is according to the description of the predicate. That description either persists with the persistence of the subject's essence; then the predicate persists with the persistence of the subject's essence, as in 'every human is an animal as long as it is an animal'—this proposition is also of the kind of absolute perpetual. Or it does not persist; then either it is...
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage classifies perpetual propositions (qadaya da'ima) based on how perpetuity (dawam) is determined. The author states that perpetuity can be absolute (unconditioned) or conditioned. The condition can stem from the subject, the predicate, or neither. Under [A] the first type: perpetuity according to the essence of the subject yields the 'absolute perpetual' where the predicate holds as long as the subject exists, e.g., 'every human is an animal perpetually'. Under [B] the second type: perpetuity according to the description of the subject. If that description persists with the essence, it reduces to the absolute perpetual. If not, then we have either (i) the 'general 'urfiya' where the predicate holds only as long as the description persists, e.g., 'every wine is intoxicating as long as it is wine' (this is also called 'absolute converted' because its negative is convertible), or (ii) the 'specific 'urfiya' where the predicate holds not always but only as long as the description, adding a non-perpetuity condition. Under [C] the third type: perpetuity according to the description of the predicate, similarly leading to absolute perpetual if the description persists with the subject's essence, otherwise further subdivisions (cut off in text). This classification is crucial for understanding modal conversions and syllogistic moods in Islamic logic.

دائمة مطلقة
عرفية

Key Takeaway
Perpetual propositions are classified by whether the perpetuity is absolute or conditioned by a description of the subject or predicate, and each type has distinct logical properties.

Three Definitions of the Absolute Proposition
Author presents and critiques three opinions on the definition of absolute propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three definitions of the absolute proposition and evaluate the weakness of the third opinion.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition is defined differently; the third opinion is critically weak.

This passage presents three competing definitions of the absolute (mutlaqa) proposition in Islamic logic. The first two are widely discussed, while the third is sharply criticized by the author. Understanding these definitions is crucial for correctly interpreting modal propositions in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first opinion
The first opinion defines the absolute proposition as one from which modality is omitted in both wording and conception. The example given is 'Every human is an animal' – even though it is actually necessary, we ignore the modality.
This opinion focuses on the form of the proposition, not its underlying reality.
2
Understand the second opinion
The second opinion defines the absolute proposition as one where the predication holds at some time, not necessarily for the entire duration of the subject's existence or for every individual.
This introduces a temporal element into the definition.
3
Examine the third opinion
The third opinion requires the subject to actually exist at some time. For example, 'Every white' means every white thing exists actually at some time.
This opinion ties the proposition to actual existence.
4
Analyze the author's critique
The author calls the third opinion 'very weak' because without an explicit time condition, the predicate becomes more general than the subject, leading to logical problems.
The critique points out that some propositions have subjects whose existence is not considered.
5
Compare the definitions
The first two definitions are more accepted, while the third is rejected. Note how each handles the example 'Every human is an animal' differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإطلاق العام : فإنه من جملة ما فيه من الكلام بأنه ما هو وكيف هو : [1] منهم من قال إن المطلقة من القضايا هي التي تحذف عنها الجهة قولاً وتصورا بوجه لا يلتفت إلى الجهة يجب لها في التصور حتى إن قولنا «كل إنسان حيوان قضية مطلقة وإن كان في الحقيقة أن الحيوان موجود لكل ما هو إنسان ما دامت ذاته موجودة؛ فلا يلتفت إلى ذلك بل إلى ما يشارك فيه هذه القضية غيرها وهو أن الحيوان موجود للإنسان فهو من حيث إنه موجود فقط موجبة مطلقة ومن حيث إنه ضروري أو دائم فهو أخص فيكون المطلق أعم من الضروري والدائم ونحوهما. [ب] ومنهم من قال إن المطلقة هي التي لا يكون الحمل فيه بـ «إنما أو بـ «ما» ، لا يجب ذلك في كل واحد وإن اتفق في البعض بل ما يكون الحمل وقتا ما، أو لا يجب أن يكون ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودًا [ت] ومنهم من قال إنها هي التي تكون موضوعاته حاصلة بالفعل . في زمان ما حتى يكون قولنا كل أبيض معناه أن كل أبيض موجود بالفعل في زمان ما [1] فتكون المطلقة الموجبة الكلية على الرأي الأول معناه وما ذكرناه. [ب] وعلى الرأي الثاني معناه كل واحد واحد مما يوصف في الذهن بالفعل أنه «أ» دائمًا أو غير دائم فهو موصوف بأنه «ب» وقتا ما لا محالة: سواء كان معناه معينًا أو غير معين. [ت] وعلى الرأي الثالث أن كل واحد من الأشياء الموجودة في وقت ما «أ» إنه «ب» في ذلك الوقت. وهذا الرأي الثالث سخيف جدا : فإن كل واحد من الأشياء الموجودة في وقت ما إذا لم يصرح بالشرط المذكور كان بعض ما يوصف بـ (أ) وقولك كل «ب» أعم من ذلك. وهنا من قضايا ما تكون موضوعاتها أمور لا يلتفت إلى وجودها ومع ذلك يحمل عليها محمولات لا تكون ضرورية، ولا أيضًا ممكنة بل تحمل عليها بأنها توجد لا محالة وقتا ما وقد نبع من هذا الرأى الفاسد رأى آخر في
As for the general absolute (al-itlaq al-‘amm): it is among that about which there is discussion concerning what it is and how it is. [1] Some of them said that the absolute (al-mutlaqa) among propositions is that from which modality is omitted in wording and conception, in a way that one does not pay attention to the modality that must be present in conception, such that our saying 'Every human is an animal' is an absolute proposition, even though in reality animal exists for every human as long as his essence exists; but one does not pay attention to that, rather to what this proposition shares with others, namely that animal exists for humankind. So from the perspective that it exists only, it is an affirmative absolute; and from the perspective that it is necessary or permanent, it is more specific. Thus the absolute is more general than the necessary and the permanent and the like. [B] Some of them said that the absolute is that in which the predication is not with 'innama' or with 'ma' [i.e., not restricted], it is not necessary in every single one even if it occurs in some, rather the predication is at some time, or it is not necessary that it is as long as the subject's essence exists. [T] Some of them said that it is that whose subjects are actual in a time, so that our saying 'Every white' means that every white exists actually at some time. [1] So the universal affirmative absolute according to the first opinion means what we have mentioned. [B] According to the second opinion, its meaning is: every single one of what is actually described in the mind as being 'A' always or non-permanently is inevitably described as being 'B' at some time, whether its meaning is specific or non-specific. [T] According to the third opinion: that every one of the things existing at some time as 'A' is 'B' at that time. And this third opinion is very weak: for if every one of the things existing at some time is not explicitly conditioned with the mentioned condition, then some of what is described as (A) and your saying 'every B' is more general than that. And here there are propositions whose subjects are matters whose existence is not paid attention to, yet predicates are predicated on them that are not necessary nor even possible, but rather are predicated such that they exist inevitably at some time. And from this corrupt opinion another opinion emerged about...
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage discusses three definitions of the 'absolute proposition' (al-mutlaqa) in modal logic. The first opinion (by some logicians) defines it as a proposition from which modality (al-jiha) is removed both verbally and conceptually; the example given is 'Every human is an animal', which is actually necessary but is considered absolute if we ignore the necessity and focus only on the existence of the predicate. The second opinion defines it as a proposition where the predication is not restricted (not with 'innama' or 'ma'), meaning it is not necessary for all individuals or for the entire duration of the subject's existence, but rather holds at some time. The third opinion defines it as a proposition whose subjects are actual at some time, so 'every white' means every white exists actually at some time. The author then explains the implications for universal affirmative absolute propositions under each view. He strongly criticizes the third opinion as very weak (sakhif jiddan), because without the explicit time condition, the predicate is more general than the subject, leading to problems. The author notes that this corrupt opinion gave rise to another view.

Key Terms
المطلقة
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the first opinion on the absolute proposition?
A) It is a proposition where the subject exists at some time
B) It is a proposition where the predication is not restricted
C) It is a proposition from which modality is omitted in wording and conception
D) It is a proposition that is always necessary

Key Takeaway
The absolute proposition is not a single concept; the first two definitions are standard, while the third is rejected due to logical problems.
