Two Definitions of the Absolute Proposition
Author presents first two opinions on al-mutlaqa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and contrast the first two definitions of al-mutlaqa (absolute proposition) in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition is either modality-free in conception or non-essential predication at some time.

The first two opinions on the absolute proposition offer foundational definitions. The first opinion focuses on removing all modal qualifiers, while the second emphasizes that the predication need not hold essentially. Understanding these helps you recognize unmodalized propositions in logical argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Find the term 'المطلقة' in the passage and note that it is being defined.
The term is introduced as part of the general absolute proposition.
2
Analyze the first opinion
The first definition says the absolute proposition removes the mode (جهة) in utterance and conception. For example, 'كل إنسان حيوان' is considered absolute despite being necessary in reality because we ignore the necessity and consider only existence.
This makes the absolute more general than necessary or permanent.
3
Understand the rationale
The absolute is more general because it abstracts from all modal considerations, focusing only on the existence of the predicate for the subject.
The example shows that 'human is animal' is absolute from the existence perspective, even if it is necessary in reality.
4
Analyze the second opinion
The second definition says the absolute proposition is one where the predication is not by 'إنما' (essentially) or 'ما' (what), meaning it is not necessary in every case; rather it occurs at some time, not necessarily for the whole duration of the subject's existence.
This introduces a temporal condition.
5
Compare the two
Both definitions reject essential necessity, but the first removes modality entirely while the second imposes a temporal condition.
The first is broader; the second is more specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإطلاق العام : فإنه من جملة ما فيه من الكلام بأنه ما هو وكيف هو : [1] منهم من قال إن المطلقة من القضايا هي التي تحذف عنها الجهة قولاً وتصورا بوجه لا يلتفت إلى الجهة بل يجب لها في التصور حتى إن قولنا «كل إنسان حيوان» قضية مطلقة وإن كان في الحقيقة أن الحيوان موجود لكل ما هو إنسان ما دامت ذاته موجودة؛ فلا يلتفت إلى ذلك بل إلى ما يشارك فيه هذه القضية غيرها وهو أن الحيوان موجود للإنسان فهو من حيث إنه موجود فقط موجبة مطلقة ومن حيث إنه ضروري أو دائم فهو أخص فيكون المطلق أعم من الضروري والدائم ونحوهما. [ب] ومنهم من قال إن المطلقة هي التي لا يكون الحمل فيه بـ «إنما» أو بـ «ما» ، لا يجب ذلك في كل واحد وإن اتفق في البعض بل ما يكون الحمل وقتا ما، أو لا يجب أن يكون ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودًا. [ت] ومنهم من قال إنها هي التي تكون موضوعاتها حاصلة بالفعل في زمان ما حتى يكون قولنا كل أبيض معناه أن كل أبيض موجود بالفعل في زمان ما. فتكون المطلقة الموجبة الكلية على الرأي الأول معناه وما ذكرناه. [ب] وعلى الرأي الثاني معناه كل واحد واحد مما يوصف في الذهن بالفعل أنه «أ» دائمًا أو غير دائم فهو موصوف بأنه «ب» وقتا ما لا محالة: سواء كان معناه معينًا أو غير معين. [ت] وعلى الرأي الثالث أن كل واحد من الأشياء الموجودة في وقت ما «أ» إنه «ب» في ذلك الوقت. وهذا الرأي الثالث سخيف جدا : فإن كل واحد من الأشياء الموجودة في وقت ما إذا لم يصرح بالشرط المذكور كان بعض ما يوصف بـ (أ) وقولك كل «ب» أعم من ذلك. وهنا من قضايا ما تكون موضوعاتها أمور لا يلتفت إلى وجودها ومع ذلك يحمل عليها محمولات لا تكون ضرورية، ولا أيضًا ممكنة بل تحمل عليها بأنها توجد لا محالة وقتا ما وقد نبع من هذا الرأى الفاسد رأى آخر في أمر الجهات؛ وذلك أن نلتفت في أمر الضرورة والإمكان إلى أمر القضية في أن سورها يصدق ويكذب. ونترك أمر المحمول باعتباره إلى الموصوفات بالموضوع مكان قولنا كل حيوان إنسان ممكنا إذا كان يمكن أن يتوهم وقت من الأوقات لا حيوان فيه إلا الإنسان؛ فحينئذ يصدق أن كل حيوان إنسان [١٠١ و] فتكون هذه وجودية صادقة. وقبل ذلك تكون ممكنة إذا اعتبرت من حيث إن هذا السور صادق وقتا ما وهذا أيضًا من جملة ما فيه من الفساد فإن الاعتبار في الضرورة والإمكان إنما هو بحسب مقايسة حال المحمول في الموضوع؛ وليس ذلك بحسب السور بل السور يدخل عليه بعد ذلك.
As for the general absolute [proposition]: it is part of what is said about it regarding what it is and how it is. [1] Some say that the absolute proposition is that from which the mode is removed in utterance and conception, in a way that no attention is paid to the mode, but it must be in conception such that our statement 'Every human is an animal' is an absolute proposition, even though in reality animal exists for every human as long as his essence exists; but no attention is paid to that, rather to what this proposition shares with others, which is that animal exists for human. So from the perspective that it exists only, it is an absolute affirmative, and from the perspective that it is necessary or permanent, it is more specific. Thus the absolute is more general than the necessary, the permanent, and their likes. [B] Some said that the absolute is that in which the predication is not by 'essentially' or by 'what', not necessary in every case even if it occurs in some, but rather the predication occurs at some time, nor must it be as long as the subject's essence exists. [T] Some said that it is that whose subjects are actual at some time, such that our statement 'Every white' means that every white exists actually at some time. So the universal affirmative absolute according to the first opinion means what we mentioned. [B] According to the second opinion, its meaning is: each and every thing that is actually described in the mind as 'A' permanently or non-permanently, it is described as 'B' at some time necessarily, whether its meaning is determinate or indeterminate. [T] According to the third opinion, that each one of the things existing at some time 'A' is 'B' at that time. This third opinion is very weak: for each one of the things existing at some time, if the mentioned condition is not specified, then some of what is described as (A) and your statement 'every B' is more general than that. And here among propositions are those whose subjects are things whose existence is not considered, yet predicates are predicated of them that are neither necessary nor even possible, but are predicated as existing necessarily at some time. And from this corrupt opinion emerged another opinion regarding modalities: that we pay attention in matters of necessity and possibility to the proposition's quantifier being true or false, and we leave the predicate's aspect to the descriptions by the subject, like saying 'Every animal is human is possible' if one can imagine a time when there is no animal except human; then it is true that every animal is human, so this becomes a true existential. And before that it is possible if considered from the perspective that this quantifier is true at some time. This too is among the corruption in it, for the consideration of necessity and possibility is only according to the comparison of the predicate's state in the subject; and that is not according to the quantifier, rather the quantifier enters into it afterwards.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa) by listing three opinions and then critiquing the third. The author begins by stating that the general absolute proposition is part of the discussion. The first opinion: the absolute proposition removes modality (jiha) in utterance and conception, so that a statement like 'Every human is an animal' is considered absolute even though in reality it is necessary as long as the subject exists; but the absolute aspect is that it shares existence with other propositions, making it more general than necessary or permanent. The second opinion: the absolute proposition is one where the predication is not by essential (innama) or by 'what' (ma), meaning it is not necessary in every case; rather the predication happens at some time, not necessarily for the whole duration of the subject's existence. The third opinion: the absolute proposition has subjects that are actual at some time, so 'Every white' means everything white exists actually at some time. The author then explains the consequences of each opinion: for the first and second, he gives the specific meanings of a universal affirmative absolute. For the third, he says it is very weak (sakhif jiddan) because if the condition of time is not specified, the predication becomes too general. He then points out that from this corrupt third opinion arose another corrupt opinion about modalities: one that considers the truth of the quantifier over time rather than the predicate's relation to the subject. This leads to statements like 'Every animal is human is possible' if at some time only humans are animals. The author rejects this because the assessment of necessity and possibility should be based on the predicate's state in the subject, not on the quantifier.

Key Terms
المطلقة
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The subject
B) The mode (jiha) in utterance and conception
C) The quantifier
D) The predicate

Key Takeaway
The first two definitions of the absolute proposition both avoid essential necessity, but differ in whether they remove modality entirely or impose a temporal condition.

Critique of the Third Definition of the Absolute Proposition
Author presents and critiques the third opinion on al-mutlaqa and its consequences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third definition of al-mutlaqa and explain why it is considered corrupt, as well as the resulting corrupt modal opinion.

Core Idea
The third definition, requiring actual existence at some time, is absurd and leads to a wrong theory of modality based on quantifier truth.

The third opinion defines the absolute proposition as having subjects that are actually existent at some time. The author dismisses this as very weak and corrupt. Moreover, this corrupt opinion spawns an even more problematic view of modality that focuses on quantifier truth over time rather than the predicate's relation to the subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third opinion
The third definition: the absolute proposition has subjects that are actual at some time. E.g., 'Every white' means everything white exists actually at some time.
This ties the proposition to actual existence in time.
2
Understand the weakness
The author says this is 'sakhif jiddan' (very weak) because if the time condition is not specified, the predication becomes too general: some of what is described as 'A' is actually 'B' but not necessarily at the same time.
The definition fails to distinguish between types of predication.
3
Trace the corrupt consequence
From this opinion arises a view that considers necessity/possibility based on whether the quantifier is true at some time. For example, 'Every animal is human is possible' if at some time only humans exist, making the proposition true existentially.
This mistakenly assesses modality by quantifier truth over time.
4
Identify the error
The author argues that modality should be based on the comparison of the predicate to the subject (مقايسة حال المحمول في الموضوع), not on the quantifier (السور). The quantifier enters afterwards.
The corrupt view inverts the proper order.
5
Apply the critique
Using the author's critique, explain why 'Every animal is human is possible' is flawed: it treats a temporal coincidence of subjects as a modal relation.
The correct assessment would examine whether 'human' is essential or possible for 'animal'.

The Two Interpretations of Aristotle's Absolute Proposition
Author accepts first two opinions, explains Aristotle's division, and historical split.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general absolute and the existential non-absolute interpretations of Aristotle's classification of propositions.

Core Idea
Aristotle's division of propositions is ambiguous, sparking a lasting interpretative split.

This passage validates two earlier opinions on the absolute proposition and traces the historical disagreement between Themistius and Alexander of Aphrodisias. Understanding this divergence is key to grasping how modal logic developed in the Islamic tradition, especially in discussions of propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify Aristotle's three-fold division
Locate in the Arabic where Aristotle divides propositions into absolute, necessary, and possible (القضايا إما مطلقة أو ضرورية أو ممكنة).
This is the starting point for the ambiguity.
2
Recognize the two aspects of the division
The author presents two ways to interpret the division. First: absolute vs. modal (with modal split into necessary and possible). Second: actual vs. potential (with actual split into necessary and non-necessary existence).
The first makes 'absolute' the general category; the second makes it the non-necessary existential.
3
Contrast the key terms
Note the difference between 'المطلق العام' (general absolute, including necessary and possible) and 'المطلق الغير ضروري' (non-necessary absolute, existential without necessity).
The latter is more specific.
4
Map the historical positions
Themistius and his followers adopted the general absolute interpretation; Alexander and his followers adopted the existential non-necessary interpretation.
This split explains later divergences in modal logical rules.
5
Reflect on consequences
Understand that this ambiguity directly affects how propositions are classified and converted in syllogisms.
Different schools apply conversion rules differently based on their interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرأى الأول والثاني فلا مناقشة فيهما فإن من الجائز أن يعنى بالمطلق ما يصح أن يعنى به بعد أن يحفظ بأنه ما هو، وبالمطلق يذكر ويراد به المطلق الغير الضروري؛ وهذا المطلق أخص من المطلق العام الذي يعمه وغيره لا محالة. وقد قيل في إطلاق لفظ المطلق عليه أن المعلم الأول ذكر في التعليم الأول القضايا إما مطلقة أو ضرورية أو ممكنة» (١) ، وهذه القسمة قد تكون على الوجهين: أحدهما أن يقال القضية إما مطلقة وإما موجهة. والموجهة إما ضرورية وإما ممكنة، وعلى هذا الوجه تكون المطلقة هي العامة. والثاني أن يقال القضية إما أن يكون الحكم فيها بالفعل أو بالقوة وهي الإمكان. وما بالفعل يكون إما بالضرورة أو بالوجود الخالي عن الضرورة، وتكون المطلقة بحسب هذه القسمة هي الوجودية اللا ضرورية. والأمثلة المطلقة في التعليم الأول كانت مناسبة لكل واحد من الاعتبارين فلأجل هذين الاحتمالين اختلف أصحاب المعلم الأول بعده في القضية المطلقة؛ فـ«ثامطيوس» ومن وافقه حملوها على العامة الشاملة للضرورية وغيرها. والإسكندر ومن تابعه حملوها على الوجودية اللا ضرورية. (١) أنا لوطيقا الأولى لأرسطو طاليس، ص۲۱۲.
As for the first and second opinions, there is no dispute about them. For it is permissible that by 'absolute' is meant what is valid to mean after preserving that it is what it is, and by 'absolute' is meant and intended the non-necessary absolute. And this absolute is more specific than the general absolute which includes it and others necessarily. And it was said regarding the application of the term 'absolute' to it that the First Teacher mentioned in the First Teaching: 'Propositions are either absolute, necessary, or possible' (1). This division can be in two aspects: one is that it is said the proposition is either absolute or modal, and the modal is either necessary or possible; according to this aspect, the absolute is the general [one]. The second is that it is said the proposition is either actual in judgment or potential, which is possibility. What is actual is either by necessity or by existence devoid of necessity, and according to this division, the absolute is the existential non-necessary. The absolute examples in the First Teaching were suitable for each of the two considerations; because of these two probabilities, the followers of the First Teacher after him differed regarding the absolute proposition. Thamistiyus (Themistius) and those who agreed with him interpreted it as the general [absolute] that includes the necessary and others. And Iskandar (Alexander) and those who followed him interpreted it as the existential non-necessary. (1) Aristotle's Prior Analytics, p. 212.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage validates the first two opinions on the absolute proposition, noting that there is no dispute about them. The author explains that the term 'absolute' can legitimately mean either the general absolute or the non-necessary absolute, the latter being more specific. He then references Aristotle (the First Teacher) who divided propositions into absolute, necessary, and possible. This division is ambiguous and can be understood in two ways. First interpretation: propositions are either absolute or modal, and modal is further divided into necessary and possible; here 'absolute' is the general category covering both modal types. Second interpretation: propositions are either actual in judgment (which is either necessary or non-necessary existence) or potential (possible); here 'absolute' means the existential non-necessary. Because of this ambiguity, Aristotle's followers differed: Themistius and his followers held that 'absolute' means the general absolute (including necessary and possible), while Alexander of Aphrodisias and his followers held that it means the existential non-necessary. This historical note clarifies the roots of the earlier opinions.

المطلق العام
المطلق الغير الضروري
المعلم الأول

Propositions
intermediate
10 min
The Absolute Proposition and Its Two Interpretations: Themistius vs Alexander
Author examines the ambiguity of the absolute proposition and the two schools of interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of the absolute proposition (general vs special) and trace them to the two ways of dividing propositions discussed by Aristotle.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition is not unambiguous; Themistius and Alexander read Aristotle differently, leading to two distinct definitions.

In this passage, the author reveals that the simple term 'absolute' hides a controversy. By understanding the two divisions of propositions, you will see why later logicians disagreed and how each interpretation leads to a different classification of modal statements.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the irrelevance of quantifiers
The author states that necessity and possibility are not determined by the quantifier's truth or falsity, but by the relation of the predicate to the subject. Note the example: 'Every animal is human possibly' could be true at a time when only humans exist, but that is an existential, not modal, consideration.
This step clarifies that modality is about the predicate's connection, not the quantifier's scope.
2
Identify the two divisions of propositions
The passage presents two ways to divide propositions: (1) into absolute and modal, where modal is further divided into necessary and possible, making absolute the general category; (2) into actual and potential, where actual is divided into necessary and non-necessary existential, making absolute the specific non-necessary existential.
These divisions are the root of the ambiguity.
3
Understand the resulting meanings of 'absolute'
From the first division, 'absolute' means the general category including necessary and possible. From the second, 'absolute' means only the existential (actual) that is not necessary. One is broader, the other narrower.
The same term 'absolute' thus has two senses.
4
Connect to Themistius and Alexander
Because Aristotle's examples fit both divisions, Themistius adopted the general sense (absolute includes necessary), while Alexander adopted the specific sense (absolute excludes necessity). The passage explains this divergence as stemming from the two possible readings.
This historical disagreement illustrates the practical impact of the ambiguity.
5
Refine the general absolute's relation to possibility
The author clarifies that the general absolute does not include possible propositions in the same way, because possible propositions involve potential judgments, while absolute propositions concern actual judgments. The general absolute refers to actual judgments, not potential ones.
This nuance prevents overextension of the general absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمر الجهات؛ وذلك لن نلتفت في أمر الضرورة والإمكان إلى أمر القضية في أن سورها يصدق ويكذب. ونترك أمر المحمول باعتباره إلى الموصوفات بالموضوع مكان قولنا كل حيوان إنسان ممكنا إذا كان يمكن أن يتوهم وقت من الأوقات لا حيوان فيه إلا الإنسان؛ فحينئذ يصدق أن كل حيوان إنسان فتكون هذه وجودية صادقة. وقبل ذلك تكون ممكنة إذا اعتبرت من حيث إن هذا السور صادق وقتا ما وهذا أيضًا من جملة ما فيه من الفساد فإن الاعتبار في الضرورة والإمكان إنما هو بحسب مقايسة حال المحمول في الموضوع؛ وليس ذلك بحسب السور بل السور يدخل عليه بعد ذلك. وأما الرأى الأول والثاني فلا مناقشة فيهما فإن من الجائز أن يعنى بالمطلق ما يصح أن يعنى به بعد أن يحفظ بأنه ما هو، وبالمطلق يذكر ويراد به المطلق الغير الضروري؛ وهذا المطلق أخص من المطلق العام الذي يعمه وغيره لا محالة. وقد قيل في إطلاق لفظ المطلق عليه أن المعلم الأول ذكر في التعليم الأول القضايا إما مطلقة أو ضرورية أو ممكنة، وهذه القسمة قد تكون على الوجهين: أحدهما أن يقال القضية إما مطلقة وإما موجهة. والموجهة إما ضرورية وإما ممكنة، وعلى هذا الوجه تكون المطلقة هي العامة. والثاني أن يقال القضية إما أن يكون الحكم فيها بالفعل أو بالقوة وهي الإمكان. وما بالفعل يكون إما بالضرورة أو بالوجود الخالي عن الضرورة، وتكون المطلقة بحسب هذه القسمة هي الوجودية اللا ضرورية. والأمثلة المطلقة في التعليم الأول كانت مناسبة لكل واحد من الاعتبارين فلأجل هذين الاحتمالين اختلف أصحاب المعلم الأول بعده في القضية المطلقة؛ فـ«ثامطيوس» ومن وافقه حملوها على العامة الشاملة للضرورية وغيرها. والإسكندر ومن تابعه حملوها على الخاصة الخالية عن الضرورة. ثم المطلقة العامة كقولنا إن كذا كذا بالإطلاق العام يوهم أنها تعم الممكنة كما تعم غيرها من القضايا المذكورة : وليس كذلك فإنها من حيث هي هي متناولة لما يكون مشتملا على حكم يحصل بالفعل لا ما يكون مشتملا على حكم لم يحصل إلا بالقوة لكن يعرض لها أعني الممكنة ولغيرها لما أنها تقابل المطلقة بحسب ما فيها من الزوائد وإن لم يدخل الكل تحتها. ولتعلم أن للقضية من حيث هي القضية أحكامًا، ومن حيث هي قضية يوجد فيها الحمل دائمًا أو لا دائما أحكام أخص. والأمثلة في التعليم الأول تدل على أن المطلقة بحسب الاصطلاح الأول هي العامية.
Regarding modalities; we do not pay attention in the matter of necessity and possibility to the matter of the proposition in that its quantifier is true or false. And we leave the matter of the predicate according to its consideration to the descriptions with the subject, in place of our saying 'Every animal is human possibly' if it is possible to imagine a time at which there is no animal except human; then at that time it is true that every animal is human, so this is an affirmative existential. And before that it is possible if considered insofar as this quantifier is true at some time, and this also is among the corruption in it, for the consideration in necessity and possibility is only according to comparing the state of the predicate in the subject; and that is not according to the quantifier, but the quantifier enters into it after that. As for the first and second opinions, there is no dispute in them, for it is possible that by 'absolute' is meant what is valid to be meant by it after preserving what it is, and by 'absolute' it is mentioned and intended is the absolute non-necessary; and this absolute is more specific than the general absolute which includes it and other than it necessarily. And it has been said regarding the application of the term 'absolute' to it that the First Teacher mentioned in the First Teaching that propositions are either absolute or necessary or possible, and this division may be in two ways: one of them is that it is said the proposition is either absolute or modal. And the modal is either necessary or possible, and in this way the absolute is the general. The second is that it is said the proposition is either that the judgment in it is in actuality or in potentiality, and it is possibility. And what is in actuality is either by necessity or by existence devoid of necessity, and the absolute according to this division is the existential non-necessary. And the absolute examples in the First Teaching were suitable for each of the two considerations, and because of these two possibilities the followers of the First Teacher after him differed regarding the absolute proposition: Themistius and those who agreed with him carried it to the general which includes the necessary and others. And Alexander and those who followed him carried it to the specific devoid of necessity. Then the general absolute, like our saying 'such and such' with the general application, suggests that it includes the possible just as it includes other mentioned propositions; but it is not so, for it in itself concerns what includes a judgment that obtains in actuality, not what includes a judgment that obtains only in potentiality, but the possible and others occur to it because they correspond to the absolute according to what is in them of additions, even if not all are under it. And know that the proposition as such has rulings, and insofar as it is a proposition in which the predication exists always or not always, more specific rulings. And the examples in the First Teaching indicate that the absolute according to the first terminology is the general.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory discussion of the absolute proposition (al-muṭlaqa) in modal logic. The author begins by explaining how necessity and possibility are considered: they relate to the comparison of the predicate to the subject, not to the quantifier. He gives an example: 'Every animal is human possibly' could be true at a time when only humans exist, but that is an existential, not modal consideration. He then presents two ways to divide propositions: (1) into absolute and modal, where modal divides into necessary and possible, making absolute the general category; (2) into actual and potential, where actual divides into necessary and non-necessary existential, making absolute the non-necessary existential. Because the First Teacher's (Aristotle) examples fit both, Themistius and Alexander disagreed: Themistius took absolute as general (including necessary), Alexander as specific (excluding necessary). The author then clarifies that the general absolute does not include possible propositions in the same way, since possible propositions involve potential judgments. He concludes that the absolute in the first terminology is the general one.

الضرورة
الإمكان

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following propositions according to the two interpretations of the absolute proposition: (a) 'Allah is all-powerful' (necessary), (b) 'The world is contingent' (possible), (c) 'Muhammad (SAW) is a prophet' (factual). Determine which sense of 'absolute' (general or special) includes each, and explain your reasoning.
?
In a debate about divine attributes, a mutakallim says 'Allah is knowing' as an absolute proposition. If he means it in the general sense (Themistius), what does that imply about the modality of the proposition? If in the special sense (Alexander), what changes? Write a brief response comparing the two interpretations.
